Two Scenarios: Celestial Motion Needs a Mover
Al-Razi presents two scenarios for celestial motion and deduces the need for a mover.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses two possible scenarios for celestial bodies (created and moved, or pre-existing and set in motion) to argue that they necessarily require a mover and director.

Core Idea
Whatever the origin of celestial motion, it demands a mover.

Al-Razi begins his proof for God's existence by considering two possibilities: either the celestial bodies were created and then moved, or they existed motionless and were later set into motion. In both cases, reason points to a director and mover. This lesson focuses on that foundational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the two conditional scenarios and the repeated conclusion.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Find where Al-Razi presents the first scenario (bodies non-existent then existed and moved) and the second (bodies existed but stationary then moved).
The word 'أما' introduces each scenario.
3
Analyze the rational conclusion
For each scenario, notice how sound reason ('العقل') testifies to the need for a mover and director. Reflect on why a body cannot move itself.
4
Reflect on the universality
The argument is designed to work for both a created and an eternal universe (with a beginning of motion). Consider how this makes the proof flexible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأجسام بأسرها معدومة ثم وجدت وتحركت ، أو يقال : الأجسام وإن كانت موجودة إلا أنها كانت واقفة ساكنة ، فلما جاء ذلك الوقت أحدث في حركاتها السريعة ، بعضها إلى المشرق وبعضها إلى الغرب ، وبعضها إلى الشمال ، وبعضها إلى الجنوب. وعلى كلا التقديرين تصريح العقل يشهد بافتقارها إلى إله يحركها ويديرها . وأما إن قلنا : إنها كانت معدومة محضة ثم وجدت وتحركت ، فالعقل يقضي بافتقارها إلى الموجد، وأما إن قلنا : إنها كانت موجودة ، ولكنها كانت واقفة ثم ابتدأت من ذلك الوقت بالحركة فإن العقل يقضي بافتقارها إلى المحرك والمدير ، فثبت أن حركات الأفلاك تدل على جميع الاعتبارات ، على افتقارها إلى مدير يدبرها ومحرك يحركها . واعلم أن الفلاسفة . قالوا : حركات الأفلاك لا أول لها ، فوجب افتقارها إلى محرك يحركها ، ومدير يدبرها والمتكلمون قالوا : حركات الأفلاك لها أول . فوجب افتقارها إلى محرك ومدبر . ويمكن أيضاً أن يقال : هذه الحركات إما أن يقال : إنه لا أول لها ، وإما أن يقال : لها أول أيضا فإن قلنا : إنه لا أول لها وجب افتقارها إلى الإله الذي ليس بجسم ولا بجسماني بالطريق الذي ذكره الفلاسفة ، وإن قلنا : إن تلك الحركات لها بداية ، ولها أول ، وجب أيضاً افتقارها إلى المؤثر المحرك بالطريق الذي ذكره المتكلمون فظهر أن على جميع التقديرات لابد من الاعتراف بوجود إله لهذا العالم يبدبره ويتصرف فيه كيف شاء وأراد : ( ألا له الخلق والأمر تبارك الله رب العالمين ) وبالله التوفيق
Bodies in their entirety were non-existent then they existed and moved, or it is said: bodies, even if they were existent, were stationary and motionless, and when that time came, He initiated rapid movements in them, some to the east and some to the west, and some to the north and some to the south. And upon both suppositions, sound reason testifies to their need for a god (ilāh) who moves and directs them. And as for if we say: they were purely non-existent then they existed and moved, then reason judges their need for the originator (al-mūjid). And as for if we say: they were existent but were stationary and then began at that time to move, then reason judges their need for the mover (al-muḥarrik) and the director (al-mudabbir). So it is established that the motions of the spheres (ḥarakāt al-aflāk) indicate in all respects their need for a director who manages them and a mover who moves them. And know that the philosophers (al-falāsifa) said: the motions of the spheres have no beginning, so necessarily they need a mover who moves them and a director who manages them. And the theologians (al-mutakallimūn) said: the motions of the spheres have a beginning, so necessarily they need a mover and a manager. And it can also be said: these motions — either it is said that they have no beginning, or it is said that they have a beginning. For if we say that they have no beginning, then necessarily they need the deity (al-ilāh) who is not a body (jism) nor corporeal (jismānī) by the way mentioned by the philosophers. And if we say that those motions have a beginning and a start, then necessarily they need the effective mover (al-muʼaththir al-muḥarrik) by the way mentioned by the theologians. So it appears that upon all suppositions, one must acknowledge the existence of a god (ilāh) for this world who manages it and disposes in it as He wills and desires: (Indeed, His is the creation and the command; blessed is Allah, Lord of the worlds) (Quran 7:54). And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of God based on the motion of celestial bodies. The author first considers two scenarios: either the bodies were non-existent and then came into existence and moved, or they were existent but stationary and then began to move. In both cases, reason testifies that they need a mover and director. He then addresses the disagreement between philosophers and theologians: philosophers claim celestial motions have no beginning, requiring a non-corporeal mover; theologians claim they have a beginning, requiring a creator. The author concludes that regardless of which view one takes, all roads lead to the necessity of acknowledging a God who manages the world. The argument is structured to be universally persuasive, using the observable fact of motion as a starting point.

Key Terms
حركات الأفلاك
إله
محرك
مدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument for a mover only works if the universe has a beginning.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that even if the universe is eternal, the fact that motion begins at some point (or is controlled) still requires a mover.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does sound reason testify in both scenarios?
A) The bodies are eternal.
B) The bodies are self-moving.
C) The bodies need a god who moves and directs them.
D) The bodies are composed of atoms.

Key Takeaway
Celestial motion, whether starting from non-existence or from stillness, rationally requires a mover and director.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen how reason, no matter the starting point, leads to the conclusion that everything depends on God. Is there any part of your life where you act as if you are self-sufficient, as if your movement—your decisions, your achievements, your habits—does not require God's constant involvement? What would it look like today to consciously acknowledge Him as the direct mover of every action you take?
?
Al-Razi's argument is universal: it works whether you believe the universe began or not. Does your own sense of dependence on God go deep enough to cover not only your existence but every motion of your heart and body? Where do you still trust in secondary causes more than the First Cause? Write down one specific area and commit to recognizing God's direct role in it this week.
?
If your own 'motion'—your ability to pray, to work, to love—is not self-generated but given by God moment by moment, what does that mean for your humility and gratitude? Spend one minute in silence, and then make a list of five things you 'did' today that you now realize God actually enabled. Let that awareness change how you begin tomorrow.

Philosophers and Theologians Agree on a Mover
Al-Razi demonstrates that philosophers and theologians both require a mover for celestial motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi shows that both philosophers (who believe celestial motions are eternal) and theologians (who believe they have a beginning) agree that these motions require a mover and director, thus proving God's existence.

Core Idea
Whether eternal or created, motion demands a mover.

In the second part of the proof, Al-Razi addresses the famous disagreement between philosophers and theologians. Despite their conflicting views on whether celestial motions have a beginning, both groups ultimately affirm the need for a mover and director. This lesson explores their agreement and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. Note the positions of the philosophers ('الفلاسفة') and the theologians ('المتكلمون').
2
Identify each group's view
Find the exact statements: philosophers say motions have no beginning; theologians say they have a beginning. Both conclude a mover is needed.
3
Analyze the common conclusion
Even though they disagree on eternity vs. creation, both groups agree on the need for a mover and director. See how Al-Razi frames this as a unanimous proof.
4
Reflect on the final quote
The passage ends with a Quranic verse. Consider why Al-Razi chooses this verse and how it reinforces the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأجسام بأسرها معدومة ثم وجدت وتحركت ، أو يقال : الأجسام وإن كانت موجودة إلا أنها كانت واقفة ساكنة ، فلما جاء ذلك الوقت أحدث في حركاتها السريعة ، بعضها إلى المشرق وبعضها إلى الغرب ، وبعضها إلى الشمال ، وبعضها إلى الجنوب. وعلى كلا التقديرين تصريح العقل يشهد بافتقارها إلى إله يحركها ويديرها . وأما إن قلنا : إنها كانت معدومة محضة ثم وجدت وتحركت ، فالعقل يقضي بافتقارها إلى الموجد، وأما إن قلنا : إنها كانت موجودة ، ولكنها كانت واقفة ثم ابتدأت من ذلك الوقت بالحركة فإن العقل يقضي بافتقارها إلى المحرك والمدير ، فثبت أن حركات الأفلاك تدل على جميع الاعتبارات ، على افتقارها إلى مدير يدبرها ومحرك يحركها . واعلم أن الفلاسفة . قالوا : حركات الأفلاك لا أول لها ، فوجب افتقارها إلى محرك يحركها ، ومدير يدبرها والمتكلمون قالوا : حركات الأفلاك لها أول . فوجب افتقارها إلى محرك ومدبر . ويمكن أيضاً أن يقال : هذه الحركات إما أن يقال : إنه لا أول لها ، وإما أن يقال : لها أول أيضا فإن قلنا : إنه لا أول لها وجب افتقارها إلى الإله الذي ليس بجسم ولا بجسماني بالطريق الذي ذكره الفلاسفة ، وإن قلنا : إن تلك الحركات لها بداية ، ولها أول ، وجب أيضاً افتقارها إلى المؤثر المحرك بالطريق الذي ذكره المتكلمون فظهر أن على جميع التقديرات لابد من الاعتراف بوجود إله لهذا العالم يبدبره ويتصرف فيه كيف شاء وأراد : ( ألا له الخلق والأمر تبارك الله رب العالمين ) وبالله التوفيق
Bodies in their entirety were non-existent then they existed and moved, or it is said: bodies, even if they were existent, were stationary and motionless, and when that time came, He initiated rapid movements in them, some to the east and some to the west, and some to the north and some to the south. And upon both suppositions, sound reason testifies to their need for a god (ilāh) who moves and directs them. And as for if we say: they were purely non-existent then they existed and moved, then reason judges their need for the originator (al-mūjid). And as for if we say: they were existent but were stationary and then began at that time to move, then reason judges their need for the mover (al-muḥarrik) and the director (al-mudabbir). So it is established that the motions of the spheres (ḥarakāt al-aflāk) indicate in all respects their need for a director who manages them and a mover who moves them. And know that the philosophers (al-falāsifa) said: the motions of the spheres have no beginning, so necessarily they need a mover who moves them and a director who manages them. And the theologians (al-mutakallimūn) said: the motions of the spheres have a beginning, so necessarily they need a mover and a manager. And it can also be said: these motions — either it is said that they have no beginning, or it is said that they have a beginning. For if we say that they have no beginning, then necessarily they need the deity (al-ilāh) who is not a body (jism) nor corporeal (jismānī) by the way mentioned by the philosophers. And if we say that those motions have a beginning and a start, then necessarily they need the effective mover (al-muʼaththir al-muḥarrik) by the way mentioned by the theologians. So it appears that upon all suppositions, one must acknowledge the existence of a god (ilāh) for this world who manages it and disposes in it as He wills and desires: (Indeed, His is the creation and the command; blessed is Allah, Lord of the worlds) (Quran 7:54). And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of God based on the motion of celestial bodies. The author first considers two scenarios: either the bodies were non-existent and then came into existence and moved, or they were existent but stationary and then began to move. In both cases, reason testifies that they need a mover and director. He then addresses the disagreement between philosophers and theologians: philosophers claim celestial motions have no beginning, requiring a non-corporeal mover; theologians claim they have a beginning, requiring a creator. The author concludes that regardless of which view one takes, all roads lead to the necessity of acknowledging a God who manages the world. The argument is structured to be universally persuasive, using the observable fact of motion as a starting point.

?
The passage shows that even an eternal universe needs a cause. Many of us act as though our habits or achievements are eternal or self-powered. Proof from the Order of the World for a Wise Creator
Al-Razi presents a design argument based on the perfect order of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's teleological argument for the existence of God based on the perfect order and design in the world.

Core Idea
The world's perfect order cannot be by chance; it demands a wise Creator.

Al-Razi presents a second method for proving God's existence: the argument from design. He invites us to reflect on the order of the entire cosmos—both the celestial and terrestrial realms—and observe the precision. This passage outlines the argument and lists four key domains where design is evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the full meaning. Notice how al-Razi structures his argument.
The passage is dense but clear.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the statement that says the world's perfect arrangement indicates a wise governor. Reflect on why this is a logical inference.
3
Examine the four domains of evidence
The passage lists four areas from the lower world: human body, human soul, animals, plants. Note that each is said to contain wonders.
Al-Razi references his own books for detailed proofs.
4
Connect to the overall conclusion
Understand that this design argument complements other proofs and shows the necessity of a wise Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القصات الرابع والعشروت تقرير طريقة أخرى في إثبات الاله تعالى لهذا الخلاقة اعلم أن من تدبر في أجزاء العالم الأعلى والأسفل ، ظهر له أن هذا العالم مبني على الوجه الأصلح والأصوب والترتيب الأفضل والأتقن ، وصريح العقل شاهد بأن وقوع الشيء على هذا الوجه لا يكون إلا بتدبير حكيم عالم . فهذا الطريق يدل على وجود الإله لهذا العالم . ولنذكر ضوابط هذا الباب فنقول : محل الاعتبار ، إما أن يكون هو العالم الأعلى أو العالم الأسفل ، فإذا كان محل الاعتبار هو موجودات العالم الأسفل فهذا يقع على وجوه : أحدها : العجائب الكثيرة التي أودعها الله تعالى في بدن الإنسان . وتمام الكلام فيه مذكور في كتاب تشريح بدن الإنسان وثانيها : العجائب الكثيرة التي أودعها الله تعالى في روح الإنسان ونفسه ، وتمام الكلام فيه مذكور في كتاب النفس وثالثها : عجائب أحوال الحيوانات ، وتفاصيلها ، وهي مذكورة في كتاب الحيوان ورابعها : عجائب أحوال النبات، وهي مذكورة في كتاب النبات
The twenty-fourth section: Presentation of another method for proving the existence of God, exalted, for this creation. Know that whoever reflects on the parts of the upper world and the lower world, it becomes apparent to him that this world is built upon the most correct and soundest aspect and the best and most precise arrangement, and sound reason witnesses that a thing occurring in this manner cannot be except through the governance of a wise, knowing one (tadbīr ḥakīm ʻālim). So this way indicates the existence of God for this world. Let us mention the principles of this topic and say: The locus of consideration is either the upper world or the lower world. If the locus of consideration is the existents of the lower world, then this occurs in several ways: The first: the many wonders that God placed in the human body. The complete discussion of this is mentioned in the book of anatomy (Kitāb Tashrīḥ Bada al-Insān). The second: the many wonders that God placed in the human spirit and soul (rūḥ and nafs), and the complete discussion of this is mentioned in the book of the soul (Kitāb al-Nafs). The third: the wonders of the states of animals and their details, and they are mentioned in the book of animals (Kitāb al-Ḥayawān). The fourth: the wonders of the states of plants, and they are mentioned in the book of plants (Kitāb al-Nabāt).
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage introduces a second method for proving the existence of God, namely the argument from design. The author states that contemplating the parts of the upper and lower worlds reveals that the world is constructed in the most excellent and precise manner. Reason dictates that such perfection cannot result from chance but must be due to the governance of a wise and knowing being. This indicates the existence of God. The author then outlines the subdivisions of this argument: the locus of consideration can be either the upper world (celestial realm) or the lower world (terrestrial realm). Focusing on the lower world, he lists four areas where wonders (i.e., intricate design) are manifest: the human body, the human soul/spirit, the states of animals, and the states of plants. He references his other works for detailed treatment of each. The passage thus provides an overview of a teleological argument grounded in observable order and purpose.

The Design Argument for God's Existence
Author presents the design argument for God's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the teleological argument for God's existence as presented by the author, and how reflection on cosmic order rationally leads to the recognition of a wise Creator.

Core Idea
The world's perfect order points inevitably to a wise Creator.

This passage presents a concise version of the teleological or design argument. The author invites us to reflect on the universe's intricate order and then draws a rational conclusion: such precision cannot be accidental. It requires a wise and knowing being. This argument is a cornerstone of natural theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the flow from reflection to conclusion.
Key terms to watch: تدبر, صريح العقل, حكيم, عالم.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
The premise is that reflection on the world reveals optimal order. The conclusion is that this order necessitates a wise manager.
3
Understand the rational principle
The author invokes 'clear intellect' as a witness. This is a self-evident principle: such perfect order cannot come from chance.
This principle is universal, not subjective.
4
Connect to the broader proof
Consider how this argument fits into proving God's existence. It is a direct inference from observed order to a wise Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن من تدبر في أجزاء العالم الأعلى والأسفل، ظهر له أن هذا العالم مبني على الوجه الأصلح والأصوب والترتيب الأفضل والأتقن، وصريح العقل شاهد بأن وقوع الشيء على هذا الوجه لا يكون إلا بتدبير حكيم عالم. فهذا الطريق يدل على وجود الإله لهذا العالم.
Know that whoever reflects upon the parts of the higher and lower world, it becomes clear to him that this world is built upon the most correct and most sound manner and the best and most perfect arrangement, and the clear intellect testifies that a thing happening in this manner cannot be except through the management of a wise, knowing one. So this way indicates the existence of the Creator for this world.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a concise presentation of the teleological argument (design argument) for the existence of God. The author begins by stating the premise: deep reflection on all parts of the universe—both the celestial (higher) and terrestrial (lower) realms—reveals that the world is constructed in the most optimal, sound, and perfect order. Then he invokes a fundamental rational principle: the clear intellect (صريح العقل) testifies that such precise and optimal ordering cannot occur by chance or without a purposeful agent; it necessarily requires the direction of a being who is both wise (حكيم) and all-knowing (عالم). The conclusion follows directly: this line of reasoning proves the existence of the Creator for this world. The author uses the phrase 'هذا العالم' (this world) to emphasize the particularity of the argument. The proof is presented as a self-evident rational inference from observed order to a wise designer.

عالم
وجود الإله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the design argument is based on personal opinion or subjective preference.
Correction: The author grounds the argument in 'clear intellect' (صريح العقل), which is a universal rational principle, not personal opinion. The inference from order to a designer is presented as self-evident to any rational mind.

Eight Aspects of Contemplation in the Lower World
Author enumerates eight categories of wonders in the lower world for contemplating divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author of al-Matalib al-Aliyah systematically classifies the entire lower world into eight domains of wonders that point to divine wisdom.

Core Idea
From the human body to mineral interactions, every corner of the lower world reveals divine design.

This passage is a masterful classification that organizes the subject matter of contemplation in the lower world. Rather than focusing on isolated examples, the author presents eight comprehensive categories. Each category represents a distinct field of science, and together they show the depth and breadth of divine wisdom in creation. As you read, notice how the author references his own specialized works for each category.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two worlds
The passage begins by dividing all possible objects of contemplation into the higher and lower worlds. Focus on the lower world for this lesson.
The higher world is reserved for later discussion.
2
Read the eight aspects
Go through the list of eight aspects carefully. Notice the order: from humans to animals, plants, minerals, atmospheric effects, elements, and finally the interrelations.
3
Recognize the systematic nature
Each aspect corresponds to a scientific discipline. The author's cross-references show that this classification is not arbitrary but based on extensive study.
4
Appreciate the eighth aspect
The final aspect about interrelationships and mutual aid is particularly important. It shows that the design is not just in individual things but in the harmony of the whole.
5
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself why the author would take the trouble to list all these. The answer: to make the argument for divine wisdom comprehensive and irrefutable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنذكر ضوابط هذا الباب فنقول: محل الاعتبار، إما أن يكون هو العالم الأعلى أو العالم الأسفل، فإذا كان محل الاعتبار هو موجودات العالم الأسفل فهذا يقع على وجوه: أحدها: العجائب الكثيرة التي أودعها الله تعالى في بدن الإنسان. وتمام الكلام فيه مذكور في كتاب تشريح بدن الإنسان. وثانيها: العجائب الكثيرة التي أودعها الله تعالى في روح الإنسان ونفسه، وتمام الكلام فيه مذكور في كتاب النفس. وثالثها: عجائب أحوال الحيوانات، وتفاصيلها، وهي مذكورة في كتاب الحيوان. ورابعها: عجائب أحوال النبات، وهي مذكورة في كتاب النبات. وخامسها: عجائب أحوال المعادن، وهي مذكورة في كتاب المعادن؛ أعني خواصها وتأثير بعضها في بعض، وتولد العجائب والغرائب من كيفية امتزاجاتها. وكتب الأكثرين تدل على أحوال كثيرة منها. وسادسها: عجائب الآثار العلوية. وسابعها: عجائب طبقات العناصر. وهي مذكورة في كتاب صفات العناصر. وثامنها: العجائب الحاصلة بسبب المناسبات المعتبرة بين تلك الأشياء وكيفية معونة كل واحد في حفظ نوع الباقي.
Let us mention the rules of this chapter and say: The subject of contemplation is either the higher world or the lower world. If the subject of contemplation is the existents of the lower world, this occurs in aspects: First: the many wonders that Allah has placed in the human body. The complete discussion of it is mentioned in the book on human anatomy. Second: the many wonders that Allah has placed in the human spirit and soul, and the complete discussion of it is mentioned in the book on the soul. Third: the wonders of the states of animals and their details, and they are mentioned in the book on animals. Fourth: the wonders of the states of plants, and they are mentioned in the book on plants. Fifth: the wonders of the states of minerals, and they are mentioned in the book on minerals; I mean their properties and the influence of some upon others, and the generation of wonders and marvels from the manner of their mixtures. And the books of most [authors] indicate many states from them. Sixth: the wonders of the upper atmospheric effects. Seventh: the wonders of the layers of the elements. And they are mentioned in the book on the attributes of the elements. Eighth: the wonders resulting from the considered correspondences between those things and the manner of each one's assistance in preserving the type of the remaining.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the subject matter for contemplating divine wisdom in the lower world. The author first establishes the two general domains: the higher (celestial) and lower (terrestrial) worlds. He then focuses on the lower world and enumerates eight distinct aspects. Each aspect points to a specific field of study: (1) the human body (anatomy), (2) the human spirit and soul (psychology), (3) animals (zoology), (4) plants (botany), (5) minerals (mineralogy, including their properties, interactions, and compound marvels), (6) upper atmospheric phenomena (meteorology), (7) layers of elements (the basic elements of the sublunary world), and (8) the profound interrelationships and mutual aid among these various things for the preservation of each other's species. The author references his other books for detailed discussions, indicating that this is a summary framework. The purpose is to show that every domain of the lower world is filled with wondrous designs that testify to divine wisdom. The enumeration is exhaustive, and the author explicitly states the eighth aspect as the 'correspondences' (مناسبات) and mutual assistance, which is a key point in design arguments: not only are individual things well-designed, but they also fit together in a harmonious whole.

محل الاعتبار
العالم الأسفل
العجائب
مناسبات

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Forty-Five Celestial Motions
Author enumerates the forty-five celestial motions and their breakdown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author enumerates the forty-five celestial motions as evidence of optimal design.

Core Idea
The precise number of motions points to a wise designer.

Sanusi turns his attention to the celestial realm, where he identifies three aspects of study. He focuses on the motions of the planets and stars, showing that there are exactly forty-five types, each with a specific measure and direction. This detailed enumeration underscores the order in the cosmos.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own enumeration come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the three aspects
Notice that the author lists three aspects of research: natures, magnitudes, and motions. He elaborates only on the third aspect.
3
Understand the calculation of 45 motions
Follow the breakdown: 7 planets × 6 directions = 42, plus 2 for fixed stars and 1 for the encompassing orb equals 45.
The six directions are east-west, west-east, south-north, north-south, up-down, down-up.
4
Reflect on the phrase 'on the most sound and correct manner'
This phrase indicates the motions are optimally arranged, which is a key premise in the design argument.
The author uses the same phrase as in earlier arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا كان محل الاعتبار: هو العالم الأعلى، فالبحث فيه من وجوه: الأول: معرفة طبائع الأفلاك والكواكب. ثانيها: معرفة مقاديرها، وعين كل واحد منها. وثالثها: معرفة حركاتها المختلفة بحسب الطول والعرض ووقوع تلك الحركات على الوجه الأصوب والأصلح وهي خمسة وأربعون نوعا من الحركات، وذلك لأنه يعرض لكل واحد من الكواكب السبعة ست حركات إلى ست جهات مختلفة: إحداها: من المشرق إلى المغرب. وثانيها: من المغرب إلى المشرق. وثالثها: من الجنوب إلى الشمال. ورابعها: من الشمال إلى الجنوب. وخامسها: من الأعلى إلى الأسفل. وسادسها: من الأسفل إلى الأعلى. فيكون جملتها: اثنين وأربعين نوعا من الحركة، ويعرض للكواكب الثابتة حركتان، وللفلك المحيط حركة واحدة، فتلك خمسة وأربعون نوعا من الحركة، ولكل واحد منها مقدار خاص في السرعة والبطء والجهة، ولكل واحد
And if the subject of contemplation is the higher world, then research into it [proceeds] from aspects: First: knowing the natures of the orbs and planets. Second: knowing their magnitudes, and the substance of each one. Third: knowing their various motions according to longitude and latitude, and the occurrence of those motions in the most sound and most correct manner—and they are forty-five types of motions. That is because there occur for each of the seven planets six motions towards six different directions: first: from east to west. Second: from west to east. Third: from south to north. Fourth: from north to south. Fifth: from top to bottom. Sixth: from bottom to top. So their total is forty-two types of motion. And there occur for the fixed stars two motions, and for the encompassing orb one motion. That makes forty-five types of motion. And each one has a specific quantity in speed and slowness and direction, and each one...
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage outlines the aspects of contemplating the higher (celestial) world. The author mentions three main topics: (1) the natures of the orbs and planets, (2) their magnitudes and individual substances, and (3) their motions. He focuses on the third aspect, providing a detailed enumeration of the celestial motions. He states that the motions occur in the most sound and correct manner (الوجه الأصوب والأصلح), which is the key point for the design argument. Then he computes the total: each of the seven planets has six motions (east-west, west-east, south-north, north-south, up-down, down-up), totaling 42. The fixed stars have two motions, and the encompassing orb (the ninth sphere) has one motion, making 45 types. He adds that each motion has a specific measure of speed, slowness, and direction. The passage is cut off, but the enumeration is complete. The underlying argument is that such complex, precise, and optimally arranged motions cannot be accidental; they point to a wise designer. The author does not explicitly state the conclusion here, but it is implicit from the earlier general argument. The use of 'على الوجه الأصوب والأصلح' echoes the phrase from the first lesson, linking the celestial motions to the overarching design.

Correction: The author rigorously derives 45 from the seven planets' six motions (42), plus two for fixed stars and one for the encompassing orb, showing a deliberate enumeration.

The cosmos displays wonders in minerals, atmosphere, elements, and their interconnections.

This passage presents four additional wonders that reveal the wisdom of the Creator. After covering earlier types, the author turns to minerals, upper atmospheric phenomena, elemental layers, and the interrelations between them. Each category shows a different dimension of cosmic order and mutual dependence.

The eighth type is the wonders resulting from the interrelations between these things and how each helps preserve the other. This demonstrates that creation is interconnected.

The Celestial World: Three Investigations and 45 Motions
Enumerates the three investigations and calculates 45 motions, ending with teleological proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational investigations into the celestial world (natures, quantities, motions) and how the enumeration of 45 motions leads to the conclusion of a wise Designer.

Core Idea
The ordered motions of the heavens point to a wise Manager, not to the bodies themselves.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah introduces a systematic study of the celestial world. It presents three sequential investigations: the natures of spheres and stars, their quantities, and their motions. The detailed counting of forty-five types of motion leads to a powerful teleological argument for the existence of a wise and knowing Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Notice the enumeration style and the logical flow from investigation to conclusion.
The passage uses a clear enumerative structure.
2
Identify the three investigations
The passage lists three investigations: natures, quantities, and motions. Recognise how each builds on the previous.
The third investigation includes the detailed breakdown of motions.
3
Understand the counting of motions
Follow the breakdown: 7 planets × 6 directions = 42, plus 2 for fixed stars, plus 1 for outermost sphere = 45. Each motion has a specific purpose.
The six directions are given: east-west, west-east, south-north, north-south, top-bottom, bottom-top.
4
Reflect on the teleological conclusion
The author argues that these arrangements are not inherent but are by design of a wise Manager. Consider why this is a strong argument.
The passage states that if the motions were absent, the world's interests would be disrupted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : معرفة طبائع الأفلاك والكواكب. ثانيها : معرفة مقاديرها، وعين كل واحد منها. وثالثها : معرفة حركاتها المختلفة بحسب الطول والعرض ووقوع تلك الحركات على الوجه الأصوب والأصلح وهي خمسة واربعون نوعا من الحركات، وذلك لأنه يعرض لكل واحد من الكواكب السبعة ست حركات إلى ست جهات مختلفة: إحداها : من المشرق إلى المغرب. وثانيها : من المغرب إلى المشرق . وثالثها : من الجنوب إلى الشمال. ورابعها : من الشمال إلى الجنوب. وخامسها : من الأعلى إلى الأسفل. وسادسها : من الأسفل إلى الأعلى. فيكون جملتها : اثنين وأربعين نوعا من الحركة، ويعرض للكواكب الثابتة حرکتان ، وللفلك المحيط حركة واحدة فتلك خمسة وأربعون نوعا من الحركة ، ولكل واحد منها تأثير خاص، في منافع هذا العالم ، لو لم توجد لاختلت مصالح هذا العالم ، ولا شك أن هذه الترتيبات العجيبة لم تقع لذات الجسم فهي بتدبير مدبر حكيم عليم .
The first: knowledge of the natures of the spheres and the stars. The second: knowledge of their quantities, and the essence of each one. And the third: knowledge of their various motions according to longitude and latitude, and the occurrence of those motions in the most correct and beneficial manner, and they are forty-five types of motions. That is because for each of the seven planets there occur six motions towards six different directions: one of them: from the east to the west; the second: from the west to the east; the third: from the south to the north; the fourth: from the north to the south; the fifth: from the top to the bottom; the sixth: from the bottom to the top. So their total becomes forty-two types of motion, and for the fixed stars there occur two motions, and for the outermost sphere one motion, so those are forty-five types of motion. And each of them has a specific effect in the benefits of this world; if they were absent the interests of this world would be disrupted. And there is no doubt that these wonderful arrangements did not occur for the essence of the body, so they are by the design of a wise, knowing Manager.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents three investigations into the celestial world. First: the natures of spheres and stars. Second: their quantities and individual essences. Third: their motions, which are forty-five types. The author explains that each of the seven planets has six motions in different directions (east-west, west-east, south-north, north-south, top-bottom, bottom-top), giving 42 motions. Then the fixed stars have two motions, and the outer sphere has one, totaling 45. Each motion has a specific effect on the world's benefits; without them the world's order would collapse. The author concludes that these arrangements are not inherent to the bodies themselves but are by the design of a wise and knowing Manager (مدبر حكيم عليم). This is a teleological argument for God's existence and wisdom.

Key Terms
طبائع الأفلاك
مقادير الكواكب
حركات الكواكب
ست جهات
مدبر حكيم عليم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The motions of the stars and spheres are arbitrary or natural properties of the bodies themselves.
Correction: The passage argues that the wonderful arrangements and specific effects of each motion show they are not inherent to the body but are by the design of a wise, knowing Manager (مدبر حكيم عليم).

Key Takeaway

The Connection Between Celestial Motions and Earthly Phenomena
Sanusi enumerates three celestial motions and their earthly effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the daily, monthly, and yearly motions of celestial bodies produce night/day, lunar benefits, and the four seasons, all pointing to divine design.

Core Idea
Celestial motions are not random; they orchestrate earthly benefits with precision.

The fourth investigation explores the profound link between the motions of celestial bodies and the states of the lower world. Sanusi shows that each type of motion serves a distinct purpose: the daily motion brings night and day, the monthly motion through the moon brings benefits, and the yearly motion through the sun brings the four seasons. This interconnectedness reveals a wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the three motions and their effects.
2
Identify the three motions
Find the daily, monthly, and yearly motions and what each produces.
3
Reflect on the benefits
Consider why Sanusi emphasizes benefits and what this implies about purpose.
The benefits are countless, known only to Allah.
4
Connect to the overall argument
This investigation is part of a larger proof for God's existence, showing design in the cosmos.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابعها : ارتباط أحوال العالم الأسفل بأحوال حركات الأجرام العالية ، فإن بسبب الحركة اليومية يتعاقب الليل والنهار مع كثرة ما فيهما من المصالح وبسبب الحركة الشهرية الحاصلة بسبب القمر تكون المصالح وبسبب الحركة السنوية الحاصلة بسبب الشمس ، تحصل الفصول الأربعة التي لا يعرف كمية مصالحها إلا الله تعالى. وخامسها : كيفية ارتباط أحوال هذا العالم بحركة الشمس بسيرها وبيان أنه جعل الشمس سلطان عالم الأجسام وجعل سائر الكواكب كالرعية لها وربط بسيرها أحوال البحار والجبال ومواضع العمران ومواضع الخراب من الأرض.
The fourth: the connection of the states of the lower world with the states of the motions of the celestial bodies. For due to the daily motion, night and day alternate with many benefits therein; and due to the monthly motion occurring through the moon, benefits occur; and due to the yearly motion occurring through the sun, the four seasons occur, the quantity of whose benefits only Allah knows. And the fifth: how the states of this world are connected with the motion of the sun through its course, and an explanation that He made the sun the sultan of the world of bodies and made the rest of the planets like subjects to it, and tied to its course the states of the seas, mountains, places of habitation, and places of ruin on the earth.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents two more investigations. The fourth investigates the connection between the lower world and celestial motions: the daily motion causes day and night with their benefits; the monthly motion via the moon brings benefits; the yearly motion via the sun produces the four seasons whose benefits are vast and known only to Allah. The fifth investigation focuses on the sun's role: it is the sultan of the physical world, with planets as subjects. The sun's course governs the states of seas, mountains, inhabited areas, and ruined areas on earth. This highlights the profound interdependence of all creation, pointing to a single wise Creator.

Contemplating the Stars: Human Reason and the Quran's Superiority
Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents the sixth investigation: contemplation of stars and the superiority of the Quranic signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contemplation of stars reveals divine wisdom and why the Quran is the ultimate source for recognizing these signs.

Core Idea
The cosmos is a book of signs, and the Quran is its perfect commentary.

The sixth investigation invites us to reflect on the fixed and wandering stars. The author asserts that each star contains dazzling wisdom, but human intellect can grasp only a tiny fraction. This lesson explores that limitation and how the Quran surpasses all other books in presenting these divine proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once, then its English translation. Let the author's own words set the context before analysis.
2
Identify the two types of stars
The passage mentions 'fixed stars' and 'wandering stars'. Notice that 'wandering stars' refers to planets. Reflect on why both categories are included.
This classification is traditional in Islamic astronomy.
3
Reflect on 'a sea without a shore'
Consider what the author means by calling this research an endless sea. What does this imply about the pursuit of knowledge through contemplation of creation?
It emphasizes both the vastness and the limits of human reason.
4
Examine the Quranic verse
Read verse 2:164 quoted in the passage. List the signs it enumerates and note how each points to divine power and wisdom.
The verse ends with 'signs for a people who use reason', linking reason and revelation.
5
Connect: Reason and Revelation
Synthesize the lesson: human reason can only know a little from contemplating stars, but the Quran provides complete exposition. Why does the author stress this?
This balances rational inquiry with reliance on divine guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسادسها : التأمل في أحوال الكواكب الثابتة، وفي أحوال الكواكب السيارة. واليقين حاصل بأنه تعالى أودع في كل واحد منها حكما باهرة، وأسرارا عجيبة ولا سبيل للعقول البشرية إلى معرفتها إلا في القليل القليل. واعلم أن هذا النوع من البحث بحر لا ساحل له، وليس في شيء من الكتب بيان هذا النوع من الدلائل كما في القرآن، فإنه مملوء من هذا النوع من البيان. قال الله تعالى: {إن في خلق السموات والأرض واختلاف الليل والنهار والفلك التي تجري في البحر بما ينفع الناس وما أنزل الله من السماء من ماء فأحيا به الأرض بعد موتها وبث فيها من كل دابة وتصريف الرياح والسحاب المسخر بين السماء والأرض لآيات لقوم يعقلون}.
And the sixth: contemplation of the states of the fixed stars and the states of the wandering stars. And it is certain that He, exalted, has deposited in each of them dazzling wisdom and wondrous secrets, and the human intellects have no way to know them except a very little. And know that this type of research is a sea without a shore, and there is in none of the books an exposition of this type of evidence as there is in the Quran, for it is filled with this type of exposition. Allah Almighty said: 'Indeed, in the creation of the heavens and the earth, and the alternation of the night and the day, and the ships which sail through the sea with that which benefits people, and what Allah has sent down from the heavens of rain, giving life thereby to the earth after its lifelessness and dispersing therein every [kind of] moving creature, and [His] directing of the winds and the clouds controlled between the heaven and the earth are signs for a people who use reason.'
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth investigation: contemplation of both fixed and wandering stars. The author asserts certainty that Allah has placed dazzling wisdom and wondrous secrets in each star, but human intellects can only know a tiny fraction. He then notes that this type of research (investigating cosmic signs) is an endless sea. No book explains these proofs as the Quran does, for the Quran is filled with such exposition. He quotes verse 2:164, which enumerates signs in creation, the alternation of night and day, ships, rain, life after death, animals, winds, and clouds. This passage emphasizes that while rational investigation can go far, the Quran is the ultimate source for understanding these divine signs.

The Fourth Argument: From Daily, Monthly, and Annual Motions
Explains how daily, monthly, and annual motions produce benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rhythmic cycles of celestial motions—day and night, lunar phases, and seasonal changes—reveal an intelligent Designer who provides benefits to the lower world.

Core Idea
Celestial motions are not random but serve consistent benefits, pointing to a wise Creator.

This passage introduces the fourth of six arguments from celestial phenomena. It focuses on three observable cycles—daily, monthly, and annual—and highlights the benefits that flow from each. By reflecting on these regularities, one can see that they are not accidental but designed for the welfare of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the sequential structure of the three motions.
The passage uses 'بسبب' for each motion, linking cause to benefit.
2
Identify the three motions
Distinguish the daily motion (حركة اليومية), monthly motion (حركة الشهرية), and annual motion (حركة السنوية). Notice what each produces.
3
Reflect on the benefits
Consider the benefits mentioned: night/day, lunar phases, seasons. Contemplate how these are essential for life.
The text says the benefits of seasons are known fully only to Allah, emphasizing their vastness.
4
Connect to the argument's purpose
Understand that this regular, beneficial pattern is evidence of a wise Creator who designed the cosmos.
This is a teleological argument from order and purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابعها : ارتباط أحوال العالم الأسفل بأحوال حركات الأجرام العالية ، فإن بسبب الحركة اليومية يتعاقب الليل والنهار مع كثرة ما فيهما من المصالح وبسبب الحركة الشهرية الحاصلة بسبب القمر تكون المصالح وبسبب الحركة السنوية الحاصلة بسبب الشمس ، تحصل الفصول الأربعة التي لا يعرف كمية مصالحها إلا الله تعالى
وخامسها : كيفية ارتباط أحوال هذا العالم بحركة الشمس [ بسيرها . و بيان أنه جعل الشمس ] سلطان عالم الأجسام وجعل سائر الكواكب كالرعية لها وربط بسيرها أحوال البحار والجبال ، ومواضع العمران ، ومواضع الخراب من الأرض .
وسادسها : التأمل في أحوال الكواكب الثابتة ، وفي أحوال الكواكب السيارة . واليقين حاصل بأنه تعالى أودع في كل واحد منها حكماً باهره ، وأسرارا عجيبة ولا سبيل للعقول البشرية إلى معرفتها إلا في القليل القليل
The fourth: The connection of the states of the lower world with the states of the motions of the celestial bodies. For due to the daily motion, night and day alternate with the many benefits in them; and due to the monthly motion caused by the moon, benefits occur; and due to the annual motion caused by the sun, the four seasons occur, the extent of whose benefits no one knows except Allah the Exalted.
The fifth: The manner of the connection of the states of this world with the motion of the sun [in its course. And the explanation that He made the sun] the ruler of the world of bodies and made the other planets like subjects to it, and tied to its course the states of the seas, mountains, inhabited areas, and ruined areas of the earth.
The sixth: Reflecting on the states of the fixed stars and on the states of the moving planets. And certainty is attained that the Exalted has deposited in each one of them dazzling wisdom and wondrous secrets, and there is no way for human intellects to know them except in the very least.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage enumerates three specific proofs (the fourth, fifth, and sixth in a series) from celestial phenomena for the existence and wisdom of God. The fourth argument highlights how the daily, monthly, and annual motions of celestial bodies produce regular cycles (day/night, lunar phases, seasons) that provide immense benefits to the world, demonstrating a wise Designer. The fifth argument shows the sun as a ruler over the world of bodies, with other planets as subjects, and the sun's motion determining conditions of seas, mountains, and inhabited or desolate areas on earth, indicating a purposeful order. The sixth argument calls for contemplation of the fixed stars and planets, asserting that certainty is reached that God has placed in each of them wonderful wisdom and secrets, beyond full human comprehension. These arguments are based on observable phenomena and aim to lead to recognition of divine wisdom.

حركات الأجرام العالية
مصالح

Correction: The passage emphasizes that they bring immense benefits, indicating intentional design.

B) Monthly motion
C) Annual motion

The Fifth Argument: The Sun's Rule and Its Connection to Earthly States
Describes the sun’s dominion and its connection to earthly states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sun's unique role as a ruler over all bodies and its motion's influence on earthly geography demonstrate a designed order.

Core Idea
The sun's central position and its motion regulate the entire terrestrial realm.

The fifth argument focuses on the sun as a ruler over the world of bodies, with other planets as subjects. It highlights how the sun's motion determines the states of seas, mountains, inhabited areas, and ruins on earth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage

2
Understand the sun's rulership
The sun is described as the ruler of the world of bodies, with other planets as subjects. This indicates a hierarchical ordering.
The sun's motion is given special importance.
3
Note the effects on earth
The sun's course is tied to the states of seas, mountains, inhabited and ruined areas. Reflect on how these diverse features are interrelated with solar motion.
4
Recognize the design
This interconnectedness points to a purposeful arrangement, not chance.
The argument builds on the observed order to infer a Designer.

Encourages contemplation of celestial bodies to realize divine wisdom.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
The stars and planets hold wondrous secrets that testify to God's wisdom.

The sixth argument invites contemplation of the fixed stars and the moving planets. It asserts that certainty is reached that God has placed in each of them dazzling wisdom and secrets, which human intellects can scarcely grasp.

Eight Proofs in a Single Verse
Analyzes Quran 2:164 and its eight distinct types of proofs for God's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Quran 2:164 systematically presents eight distinct types of proofs for God's existence, categorized into astronomical and elemental proofs.

Core Idea
The Quran organizes observable proofs into eight categories across a single verse.

This lesson explores a remarkable verse that encapsulates eight different types of evidence for divine power. The author shows how the Quran surpasses other books in presenting such proofs. You will see how the verse groups astronomical signs, then signs from the world of elements, plants, animals, and atmospheric phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Allow the author's own rhythm to come through before analyzing.
Notice the author's high praise for the Quran's method.
2
Identify the eight proofs
As you read the verse quoted, list the eight signs mentioned: creation of heavens and earth, alternation of night and day, ships, rain, revival of earth, beasts, winds, and clouds.
The author groups them into two main categories.
3
Understand the classification
Notice that the author classifies the first three as astronomical proofs and the remaining five as proofs from the world of elements. Compare this with the actual verse.
There is a minor discrepancy: the verse has only two explicit astronomical signs; the third may be implied or a copyist issue.
4
Reflect on the Quranic method
Consider why the author emphasizes this verse. It demonstrates that the Quran provides observable, categorized evidence for divine existence, accessible to reason.
The verse concludes with 'signs for a people who reason.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذا النوع من البحث بحر لا ساحل له ، وليس في شيء من الكتب بيان هذا النوع من الدلائل كما في القرآن ، فإنه مملوء من هذا النوع من البيان . قال الله تعالى ( إن في خلق السموات والأرض ، واختلاف الليل والنهار والفلك التي تجري في البحر بما ينفع الناس وما أنزل الله من السماء من ماء ، فأحيا به الأرض بعد موتها ، وبث فيها من كل دابة ، وتصريف الرياح والسحاب المسخر بين السماء ، والأرض لآيات لقوم يعقلون ) . فهذه الآية مشتملة على ثمانية أنواع من الدلائل . فالثلاثة الأول من الدلائل الفلكية ، وهي قوله تعالى : ( إن في خلق السموات والأرض واختلاف الليل والنهار والخمسة الباقية من الدلائل، هي دلائل عالم العناصر ، وهي قوله تعالى : ( والفلك التي تجري في البحر بما ينفع الناس ) ثم ذكـر بعـده دلائل النبات فقال : ( وما أنزل الله من السماء من ماء فأحيا به الأرض بعد موتها ثم ذكر بعده دلائل الحيوان فقال وبث فيها من كل دابة) ثم ذكر دلائل الآثار العلوية وذكر فيها نوعين : الرياح والسحاب . فقال : ( وتصريف الرياح والسحاب المسخّر بين السماء والأرض ) ولما ذكر هذه الدلائل الثمانية قال : ( لآيات لقوم يعقلون)
And know that this type of inquiry is an ocean with no shore, and there is not in any of the books an exposition of this type of proofs as in the Qur'an, for it is filled with this type of exposition. Allah the Exalted said: 'Indeed, in the creation of the heavens and the earth, and the alternation of the night and the day, and the ships which sail through the sea with that which benefits people, and what Allah has sent down from the sky of water, and thereby revived the earth after its death, and dispersed therein every beast, and the directing of the winds and the clouds controlled between the heaven and the earth are signs for a people who reason' (2:164). So this verse includes eight types of proofs. The first three are from the astronomical proofs, namely His saying: 'Indeed, in the creation of the heavens and the earth and the alternation of the night and the day' — and the remaining five proofs are the proofs of the world of elements, namely His saying: 'and the ships which sail...' Then He mentioned after that the proofs of plants, saying: 'and what Allah sent down from the sky of water and revived the earth after its death' Then He mentioned after that the proofs of animals, saying: 'and dispersed therein every beast' Then He mentioned the proofs of upper atmospheric effects and mentioned two types: the winds and the clouds, so He said: 'and the directing of the winds and the clouds controlled between heaven and earth' And when He mentioned these eight proofs, He said: 'are signs for a people who reason'.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage highlights the Quranic method of presenting proofs for God's existence and attributes. The author begins by asserting that the Quran is filled with such proofs in a way no other book can match. He then quotes Surah Al-Baqarah verse 164, which lists eight signs: creation of heavens and earth, alternation of night and day, ships sailing with benefits, rain reviving the earth, dispersal of every beast, directing winds, and clouds. The author classifies these eight into two groups: the first three as astronomical proofs (though the verse only explicitly includes creation of heavens and earth and alternation of night and day; the third, ships, is odd but he includes it as part of the first three? The text says 'first three' but only quotes two from the verse; perhaps a copyist error). The remaining five are proofs from the world of elements: ships (as part of water travel), rain, revival of earth, beasts, winds and clouds. He then explains that the verse concludes by calling these 'signs for a people who reason.' This demonstrates the Quran's comprehensive and accessible approach to tawhid proofs.

دلائل فلكية
عالم العناصر
دلائل النبات
دلائل الحيوان
الآثار العلوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran is only a book of moral guidance without systematic proofs.
Correction: The Quran systematically presents observable proofs of God's power and wisdom, as demonstrated in this verse.

A) Five
B) Eight
C) Three
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
Surah Al-Baqarah 2:164 contains eight distinct categories of proofs for God's existence, demonstrating the Quran's comprehensive argumentative method.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you were to treat the eight proofs in this verse as an exam from Allah, what score would you give yourself on truly recognizing His power through them? What is one practical step you can take to move from passive observation to active reflection on His signs?

Ja'far al-Sadiq's Proof from a Sea Experience
Ja'far al-Sadiq uses a heretic's own experience to prove the existence of a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ja'far al-Sadiq used a heretic's own experience at sea to prove the existence of a Creator.

Core Idea
Hope in crisis points to the One who alone can save.

This passage presents a powerful narrative argument. A heretic denies the Creator, but Ja'far al-Sadiq gently questions him about a terrifying storm at sea. The heretic admits that even when all human means failed, he still hoped for salvation. Ja'far seizes this admission to reveal that the object of his hope is God.

Guided Path
1
Read the dialogue carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the back-and-forth between Ja'far and the heretic. The heretic's answers gradually reveal his innate belief.
Pay attention to the heretic's silence at the end.
2
Identify the key admission
The heretic admits that even after the ship and plank failed, he still hoped for salvation. This is the crucial point Ja'far uses.
Hope implies a hoped-for being.
3
Understand Ja'far's conclusion
Ja'far concludes that the being hoped for is God, the same God who saved him. The heretic's silence confirms the argument's force.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this argument is effective: it appeals to innate recognition (fitra) and personal experience. Even a denier reveals belief in crisis.
This is not just a story; it's a logical proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: روى أن بعض الزنادقة أنكر الصانع عند جعفر بن محمد الصادق. فقال جعفر: ما حرفتك؟ فقال التجارة. فقال: هل ركبت البحر؟ قال: نعم. قال هل رأيت أهواله؟ قال: نعم. هاجت في بعض الأيام رياح هائلة، فكسرت السفن، وغرقت الملاحين، فتعلقت أنا ببعض الواح السفينة، ثم ذهب ذلك اللوح عني، فوجدت نفسي في تلاطم الأمواج حتى اندفعت إلى الساحل. فقال جعفر: مذ كان اعتمادك من قبل على السفينة والملاح، وعلى اللوح، فلما ذهبت هذه الأشياء عنك، هل أسلمت نفسك للهلاك أم كنت ترجو النجاة؟ قال: بل كنت أرجو النجاة. قال: ممن ترجوها؟ فسكت الرجل. فقال جعفر: إن إلهك هو الذي كنت ترجوه في ذلك الوقت، ونجاك من الغرق، وأوصلك إلى السلامة.
The second: It is narrated that some heretic denied the Creator in the presence of Ja'far ibn Muhammad al-Sadiq. Ja'far said: 'What is your profession?' He said: 'Trade.' He said: 'Have you ridden the sea?' He said: 'Yes.' He said: 'Have you seen its terrors?' He said: 'Yes. One day huge winds raged, broke the ships, drowned the sailors, and I clung to one of the ship's planks, then that plank left me, and I found myself amid the clashing waves until I was pushed to the shore.' Ja'far said: 'Since before your reliance was on the ship and the sailor and the plank, when these things left you, did you surrender yourself to destruction or did you hope for salvation?' He said: 'Rather, I hoped for salvation.' He said: 'From whom did you hope for it?' The man was silent. Ja'far said: 'Your God is the one whom you were hoping for at that time, and He saved you from drowning and brought you to safety.'

Commentary
This passage is a narrative argument (حكاية) using a dialogue. A heretic denies the existence of the Creator (الصانع). Ja'far al-Sadiq questions him about his profession (trade) and then about his experience at sea during a storm. The heretic admits that when the ship and plank failed, he still hoped for salvation. Ja'far asks from whom he hoped, and the man falls silent. Ja'far concludes that the one from whom he hoped is his God—the same God who saved him. This argument appeals to innate recognition (fitra) and the universal human tendency to turn to a higher power in times of extreme crisis, thereby proving the existence of a creator even to one who denies it.

The Prophet's Dialogue with Imran ibn Husayn: A Proof of Monotheism
A narrative proof of God's oneness from the Prophet's dialogue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a simple dialogue between the Prophet and a companion demonstrates the rational necessity of monotheism.

Core Idea
All blessings come from one God, so there is no god but Allah.

This lesson presents a narrative proof of God's oneness from the Prophet's interaction with Imran ibn Husayn. The Prophet uses a direct question-and-answer method to lead Imran to acknowledge that all blessings and great events come from Allah alone. This teaches us a simple but powerful argument for tawheed.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the dialogue format and the logical progression.
The passage is short but dense in argument.
2
Identify the logical structure
The Prophet first asks about the number of gods, then about the source of blessings. Imran's answer forces him to acknowledge that one God provides everything.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The Prophet concludes that since all blessings come from Allah, there is no god but Him. This is an argument from ontological dependence.
This is a direct proof of tawheed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في حكاية كلمات منقولة عن أكابر الناس في هذا الباب. الأول: روي أن النبي قال لعمران بن الحصين: «كم لك من إله؟» قال: عشرة. قال: «فمن لنعمك وكرمك والأمر العظيم الذي ينزل بك؟» قال: الله تعالى. فقال عليه السلام: «فما لك من إله إلا الله».
Chapter Two: On Narrating Words Transmitted from the Great People on This Topic. The First: It is narrated that the Prophet said to Imran ibn Husayn: 'How many gods do you have?' He said: 'Ten.' He said: 'Then who is for your blessings, generosity, and the great matter that descends upon you?' He said: 'Allah, the Exalted.' So he, peace be upon him, said: 'Then you have no god except Allah.'
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents a narrative argument for the existence and oneness of God. It is a dialogue between the Prophet and Imran ibn Husayn. The Prophet first asks how many gods Imran has, to which he replies ten. Then the Prophet asks who provides his blessings and the great matters that affect him. Imran acknowledges 'Allah, the Exalted.' The Prophet then concludes that since all these come from one God, there is no other god. The argument is from the evident fact of divine provision and control over significant events.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the argument is merely rhetorical or emotional.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The Prophet's dialogue exposed that belief in one God must be reflected in exclusive reliance and gratitude. Which of your daily habits, words, or thoughts reveals that you still have other 'gods' competing with Allah? What is the first step you will take today to eliminate that competition?

Ja'far al-Sadiq's Argument from Innate Hope
A narrative proof using a heretic's experience to argue for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the innate hope for deliverance in dire situations points to the existence of God.

Core Idea
The moment hope survives all human means, it witnesses its Creator.

This lesson presents a famous narrative from Ja'far al-Sadiq, a great scholar, who debates a heretic. By asking about a real-life crisis at sea, Ja'far uncovers the heretic's own unconscious reliance on God. The story illustrates a powerful proof for God's existence rooted in human nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the story
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp the narrative. Notice the dialogue and the heretic's experience.
2
Identify the argument
Ja'far uses the heretic's own story to lead him to acknowledge God. Pay attention to the shift from reliance on physical means to hope in an unseen power.
The key moment is when the heretic admits he hoped for deliverance after losing everything.
3
Connect to the Qur'an
Reflect on the Qur'anic verse cited (29:65) and how it supports the argument that people naturally turn to God in distress.
This shows the proof is not just logical but scriptural.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: روي أن بعض الزنادقة أنكر الصانع عند جعفر بن محمد الصادق. فقال جعفر: ما حرفتك؟ فقال التجارة. فقال: هل ركبت البحر؟ قال: نعم. قال هل رأيت أهواله؟ قال: نعم. هاجت في بعض الأيام رياح هائلة، فكسرت السفن، وغرقت الملاحين، فتعلقت أنا ببعض لوح السفينة، ثم ذهب ذلك اللوح عني، فوجدت نفسي في تلاطم الأمواج حتى اندفعت إلى الساحل. فقال جعفر: مذ كان اعتمادك من قبل على السفينة والملاح، وعلى اللوح، فلما ذهبت هذه الأشياء عنك، هل أسلمت نفسك للهلاك أم كنت ترجو النجاة؟ قال: بل كنت أرجو النجاة. قال: ممن ترجوها؟ فسكت الرجل. فقال جعفر: إن إلهك هو الذي كنت ترجوه في ذلك الوقت، ونجاك من الغرق، وأوصلك إلى السلامة. واعلم أن هذا الدليل مأخوذ من القرآن، قال تعالى: ( فإذا ركبوا في الفلك دعوا الله مخلصين له الدين ).
The Second: It is narrated that a certain heretic denied the Maker in the presence of Ja'far ibn Muhammad al-Sadiq. Ja'far said: 'What is your craft?' He said: 'Trade.' And know that this proof is taken from the Qur'an, He said: 'And when they board a ship, they supplicate Allah, sincere to Him in religion.'

Through questioning, he leads the man to recall a near-death experience at sea. The man describes how his reliance on the ship, sailor, and plank failed, yet he still hoped for deliverance. Ja'far points out that this hope was directed to a being beyond the physical, which is God. The Qur'anic verse confirms that people turn to God sincerely in such situations.

Abu Hanifa's Ship Analogy: Proof from Design
Abu Hanifa's ship analogy as a proof for the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Hanifa used a logical analogy from a ship to argue for the existence of a Creator.

Core Idea
If a ship needs a captain, the universe needs a Maker.

This lesson presents a famous story of Imam Abu Hanifa confronting materialists with a powerful analogy. He asks them to consider a ship sailing without a captain—something they immediately reject as irrational. Then he applies the same logic to the universe, forcing them to acknowledge the necessity of a Creator. This is a classic argument from design in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the story
Read the entire narrative of Abu Hanifa and the materialists. Notice the setting and the confrontation.
The story is dramatic—it shows the stakes involved.
2
Identify the analogy
The ship represents the universe. Identify the correspondences: ship -> world, captain/caretaker -> Creator.
Materialists admitted the ship needs a caretaker.
3
Understand the logical move
Abu Hanifa uses reductio ad absurdum: if a small system (ship) needs a maker, the larger system (universe) certainly does.
This is an argument a fortiori (all the more reason).
4
Connect to Quranic evidence
The passage ends by quoting two Quranic verses that support the idea that the heavens and earth stand by God's command.
This shows the argument is rooted in revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: كان أبو حنيفة سيفا على الدهرية وكانوا ينتهزون الفرصة في قتله فبينما هو قاعد في مسجده يوما إذ هجم عليه جماعة بسيوف مسلولة وهموا بقتله. فقال لهم: أجيبوني عن مسألة ثم افعلوا ما شئتم. فقالوا: هات. فقال: ما تقولون في رجل يقول لكم: إني رأيت سفينة مشحونة بالأحمال، مملوءة من الأثقال، قد احتوشها في لجة البحر أمواج متلاطمة، ورياح مختلفة، وهي في هذه الأحوال تجري مستوية، ليس لها ملاح يجريها، ولا متعهد يحفظها. هل يجوز ذلك في العقل؟ قالوا: لا، هذا شيء لا يقبله العقل، فقال: أبو حنيفة يا سبحان الله إذا لم يجز في العقل سفينة تجري من غير متعهد ولا حافظ، فكيف يجوز قيام هذه الدنيا على اختلاف أحوالها، وسعة أطرافها، وتباين أكنافها من غير صانع وحافظ؟ فبكوا جميعا، وقالوا: صدقت. واعلم أن هذه الدلالة مأخوذة من قوله سبحانه: ( ومن آياته أن تقوم السماء والأرض بأمره ) ومن قوله تعالى الله الذي رفع السموات بغير عمد ترونها.
The Third: Abu Hanifa was a sword against the materialists, and they were seizing the opportunity to kill him. One day while he was sitting in his mosque, a group attacked him with drawn swords and intended to kill him. He said to them: 'Answer me one question, then do whatever you wish.' They said: 'Present it.' He said: 'What do you say about a man who tells you: I saw a ship loaded with cargo, filled with heavy loads, surrounded in the depths of the sea by crashing waves and various winds, and in these conditions it sails steadily, having no sailor to steer it nor caretaker to preserve it. Is that intellectually permissible?' They said: 'No, this is something the intellect does not accept.' So Abu Hanifa said: 'Glory be to God! If the intellect does not permit a ship to sail without a caretaker and preserver, then how can it permit the existence of this world with its diverse conditions, vast extent, and varied territories without a Maker and Preserver?' Then they all wept and said: 'You have spoken truthfully.' And know that this indication is taken from His saying: 'And among His signs is that the heaven and earth stand by His command' and from His saying: 'Allah is the One who raised the heavens without pillars that you see.'
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is an argument from design and order in the universe, presented as a reductio ad absurdum. Abu Hanifa uses the analogy of a ship requiring a captain and caretaker. The materialists agree that a ship cannot function without guidance. Abu Hanifa then argues by analogy that the universe, which is far more complex, must have a Creator and Preserver. The Quranic verses reinforce that the heavens and earth stand by God's command.

Key Terms
سفينة
ملاح
متعهد
صانع
حافظ
دهرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is not a direct proof of a personal God but shows the impossibility of the universe being without a maker.
Correction: While the argument demonstrates the necessity of a Maker, it is indeed a proof for a personal Creator who is intelligent and powerful, as the analogy requires a caretaker with will and ability.

Checkpoint
What did the materialists admit about the ship without a captain?
A) It could still sail perfectly
B) It is intellectually possible
C) It is not intellectually acceptable
D) It is possible with strong winds

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's ship analogy is a powerful argument from design that leads to the conclusion that the universe must have a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the complexity of your own life—your body, your circumstances, your existence—can you honestly say you see it as a ship without a captain? Or do you recognize the Hand of the Maker? What would change in your daily actions if you truly felt the presence of that Captain at every moment?
?
Abu Hanifa presented this argument while facing death. If someone put a sword to your throat and asked you why you believe in God, could you articulate a clear, logical reason from the world around you? If not, what is stopping you from seeking that certainty?
?
The materialists wept when they heard the analogy. When was the last time the evidence of God's existence moved you to tears—or even to a moment of profound awe? What would it take for you to see the universe with fresh eyes today?

Al-Shafi'i's Argument from the Mulberry Leaf
An inductive proof from diverse products of the same leaf pointing to a wise Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Shafi'i's inductive argument for the existence of a wise Creator based on the diverse transformations of a single mulberry leaf.

Core Idea
One leaf, many outcomes—silk, honey, musk, waste—points to a purposeful Director, not random chance.

This lesson presents a beautiful argument attributed to Imam al-Shafi'i, using a simple observation from nature. The same mulberry leaf, when consumed by different creatures, yields vastly different products. This striking diversity from a uniform source cannot be explained by the leaf itself; it demands a wise Maker who directs each creature's internal processes. Read on to see how al-Shafi'i turns a common sight into a proof of God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Let the rhythm of the argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that the leaf is described as uniform in taste, color, smell, and dye.
2
Identify the four outcomes and their agents
List the four creatures mentioned and what each produces from the same leaf. Observe the complete diversity: silk, honey, musk, and waste.
The gazelle produces musk, a fragrant substance, while other animals excrete waste.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: can the leaf itself cause these different results? Why or why not? The passage concludes that a wise Director must be responsible.
This connects to the core idea: uniformity of input + diversity of output = external designer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: سألوا الشافعي [رضوان الله عليه] عن الدليل عن الصانع فقال: ورقة الفرصاد طعمها ولونها، وريحها، وصبغها: واحد. تأكلها دودة القز فيخرج منها الإبريسم، ويأكلها النحل فيخرج منه العسل، وتأكلها الظباء فينعقد في نوافجها المسك، وتأكلها سائر الحيوانات فتفضل مع العفونة والفساد. فالذي دبر هذه الأجسام على هذه المناهج العجيبة: هو الله سبحانه.
The Fourth: They asked al-Shafi'i, may God be pleased with him, about the proof for the Maker. He said: The leaf of the mulberry tree: its taste, color, smell, and dye are one. The silkworm eats it and produces silk, the bee eats it and produces honey, the gazelle eats it and musk congeals in its scent glands, and other animals eat it and excrete it as waste and decay. So the One who directs these bodies in these wondrous ways is Allah, the Exalted.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from the diversity of outcomes from a single input. The mulberry leaf is identical in its properties, yet different creatures transform it into vastly different products: silk, honey, musk, or waste. This points to a purposeful Creator who directs each creature's processes. The argument is inductive: the uniformity of the material and diversity of results cannot be random.

Key Terms
ورقة الفرصاد
دودة القز
إبريسم
نحل
عسل
ظباء
مسك
مناهج عجيبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the leaf itself causes these transformations.
Correction: The argument shows that the leaf is uniform, so the diversity of outcomes must come from an external director—God.

Checkpoint
What does the gazelle produce from the mulberry leaf?
A) Silk
B) Honey
C) Musk
D) Waste

Key Takeaway
The mulberry leaf's uniform nature yet diverse results across species proves the existence of a wise Creator who directs each creature's transformation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mulberry leaf is something you may have passed by countless times without a second thought. After this lesson, when you next see a leaf or any natural object, will you remember that it points to a wise Director? What will you do to ensure that remembrance transforms your daily walk with Allah?
?
Al-Shafi'i used a simple example that even a child could understand. If you had to explain the proof for Allah's existence to someone using only one everyday object, which object would you choose and why? Commit to actually sharing that proof with someone this week.
?
The proof depends on noticing uniformity and diversity together. In your own life, where do you see patterns that could only come from a purposeful Creator? Write down one specific observation today and use it as a trigger for gratitude and worship.
