The Third Proof: Abu Hanifa's Ship Analogy
Abu Hanifa's ship analogy with Quranic citations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Hanifa used a rational analogy from everyday experience to argue for the existence of God, and how this proof is rooted in the Qur'an.

Core Idea
If a ship needs a captain, the universe needs a Creator.

This lesson presents a powerful story from the life of Imam Abu Hanifa, where he faced down atheist opponents with a simple yet unassailable analogy. The proof he used, drawn from the Qur'an, demonstrates that the order and complexity of the universe cannot exist without a Maker. Prepare to see how a ship without a captain becomes a compelling argument for God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative carefully
Read the story of Abu Hanifa and the Dahriyya. Notice the dramatic setting and the question he poses.
Pay attention to the reaction of the attackers.
2
Analyze the analogy
Identify the two parts: the ship (without captain) and the universe (without Creator). Understand why the intellect rejects the first and why the second is analogous.
This is a rational analogy (qiyas).
3
Connect to the Qur'an
Note that the author states this proof is taken from the Qur'an and provides three verses. See how the story exemplifies these verses.
The verses are about calling upon God in ships and God sustaining the heavens and earth.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this analogy is effective against atheists and how it relies on the principle of causality.
The argument is simple yet profound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذا الدليل مأخوذ من القرآن ، قال تعالى : ( فإذا ركبوا في الفلك دعوا الله مخلصين له الدين ) (۱) . الثالث : كان أبو حنيفة سيفا على الدهرية وكانوا ينتهزون الفرصة في قتله فبينما هو قاعد في مسجده يوما إذ هجم عليه جماعة بسيوف مسلولة وهموا بقتله . فقال لهم : أجيبوني عن مسألة ثم افعلوا ما شئتم . فقالوا: هات . فقال : ما تقولون في رجل يقول لكم : إني رأيت سفينة مشحونة بالأحمال ، مملوءة من الأثقال ، قد احتوشها في لجة البحر أمواج متلاطمة ، ورياح مختلفة ، وهي في هذه الأحوال تجري مستوية ، ليس لها ملاح يجريها ، ولا متعهد يحفظها . هل يجوز ذلك في العقل ؟ قالوا : لا ، هذا شيء لا يقبله العقل ، فقال : أبو حنيفة يا سبحان الله إذا لم يجز في العقل سفينة تجري من غير متعهد ولا حافظ ، فكيف يجوز قيام هذه الدنيا على اختلاف أحوالها ، وسعة أطرافها ، وتباين أكنافها من غير صانع وحافظ ؟ فبكوا جميعا ، وقالوا : صدقت واعلم أن هذه الدلالة مأخوذة من قوله سبحانه : ( ومن آياته أن تقوم السماء والأرض بأمره ) (۲) ومن قوله تعالى الله الذي رفع السموات بغير عمد ترونها (۳)
Know that this proof is taken from the Qur'an, He said Most High: (When they board the ship, they call upon Allah, making their faith pure for Him) (1). The third: Abu Hanifa was a sword against the Dahriyya (atheists), and they were seizing the opportunity to kill him. While he was sitting in his mosque one day, a group attacked him with drawn swords and intended to kill him. He said to them: 'Answer me a question, then do what you wish.' They said: 'Bring it.' He said: 'What do you say about a man who says to you: I saw a ship laden with cargo, full of heavy loads, surrounded in the midst of the sea by crashing waves and various winds, and in these conditions it is sailing steadily, having no captain to steer it nor a caretaker to preserve it. Is that rationally permissible?' They said: 'No, this is something the intellect does not accept.' So Abu Hanifa said: 'Glory be to God! If it is not rationally permissible for a ship to sail without a caretaker or preserver, then how is the existence of this world with its varying conditions, its vast expanses, and its diverse regions permissible without a Maker and Preserver?' So they all wept and said: 'You have spoken the truth.' Know that this indication is taken from His saying Most High: (And among His signs is that the heaven and the earth stand by His command) (2) and from His saying Most High: Allah is the One who raised the heavens without pillars you can see (3).
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the existence of God, narrated from Abu Hanifa. It is a story in which Abu Hanifa refutes the Dahriyya (materialists) using a rational analogy. The author begins by stating that this proof is derived from the Qur'an, citing a verse about calling upon God in ships. Then the story unfolds: Abu Hanifa, facing imminent death, poses a rhetorical question to his attackers. He describes a ship sailing without a captain or caretaker; they deny its rational possibility. He then applies the same logic to the world: if a ship cannot exist without a caretaker, the universe with all its complexity cannot exist without a Creator. The attackers are convinced and weep. The author concludes by linking this indication to two more Qur'anic verses about God sustaining the heavens and earth. The proof relies on the principle of causality and the impossibility of a self-sufficient ordered system.

Correction: Abu Hanifa uses a rational analogy (قياس) from the observed world, which is a valid logical proof for the existence of a Creator.

?

?

But in your own life, where do you act as if the universe is autonomous, as if you can ignore the Captain? What specific habit or assumption needs to be confronted with this analogy?
?
The attackers wept when they heard the truth. When was the last time you felt that kind of emotional and intellectual surrender to the proof of God's existence? What is preventing you from feeling that now—not in a story, but in your own heart?

The Fourth Proof: The Mulberry Leaf Analogy
Al-Shafi'i's argument from the diverse outputs of the mulberry leaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Shafi'i's argument from design using the mulberry leaf example.

Core Idea
A single leaf producing silk, honey, musk, and waste proves a wise Creator.

Imam Al-Shafi'i presents a simple yet profound proof for the existence of God. He points to a common object—the mulberry leaf—and shows how the same leaf yields vastly different beneficial products in different animals. This diversity of outcomes from a uniform input demonstrates deliberate design.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of Al-Shafi'i's argument.
2
Identify the key observation
Al-Shafi'i notes that the mulberry leaf is identical in taste, color, smell, and dye. List the four animals and the products that come from the leaf when consumed.
The uniformity of the input contrasts with the diversity of outputs.
3
Understand the conclusion
The variety of beneficial results from a single cause cannot be due to chance. It points to an intelligent designer—God.
This is a teleological argument (design argument).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : سألوا الشافعي [ رضوان الله عليه ] (٤) عن الدليل عن الصانع فقال : ورقة الفرصاد طعمها ولونها ، وريحها ، وصبغها : واحد . تأكلها دودة القز فيخرج منها الإبريسم ، ويأكلها النحل فيخرج منه العسل ، وتأكلها الظباء فينعقد في نوافجها المسك ، وتأكلها سائر الحيوانات فتفضل مع العفونة والفساد . فالذي دبر هذه الأجسام على هذه المناهج العجيبة : هو الله سبحانه .
The fourth: They asked al-Shafi'i (may God be pleased with him) (4) about the proof for the Maker, and he said: The mulberry leaf (the berry of the black mulberry) has one taste, one color, one smell, and one dye. The silkworm eats it and produces silk; the bee eats it and produces honey; the gazelles eat it and musk coagulates in their musk pods; and other animals eat it and excrete it with decay and corruption. So the One who designed these bodies in these wondrous ways is God Most High.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof, an argument from design attributed to Imam al-Shafi'i. He focuses on a single object—the mulberry leaf—and observes that despite being identical in taste, color, smell, and dye, it yields vastly different products when consumed by different creatures. Silkworms produce silk, bees produce honey, gazelles produce musk, and other animals produce waste. This diversity of outcomes from a single input demonstrates purposeful design and planning, which can only be attributed to a wise Creator. The proof is based on the principle that a uniform cause producing varied, beneficial effects requires an intelligent agent directing the processes.

Key Terms
الدليل
الصانع
ورقة الفرصاد
الإبريسم
العسل
المسك
المناهج العجيبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies on the mulberry leaf itself being special or miraculous.
Correction: The proof is not about the leaf's intrinsic qualities, but about the diversity of beneficial outcomes from a single, uniform input, which points to an intelligent designer.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Shafi'i's proof, what is the single entity that produces different beneficial results in different animals?
A) The date palm
B) The mulberry leaf
C) The olive tree
D) The wheat grain

Key Takeaway
The diverse products from a single mulberry leaf point to a purposeful Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The leaf itself does not cause the silk or honey; the transformation happens within the animal. Similarly, your external circumstances do not determine your spiritual state; what you take in and how you process it matters. What 'mulberry leaves' are you consuming daily—what inputs shape your heart and mind—and what are you producing: something beautiful like silk or something wasted like decay?
?
Al-Shafi'i points to a single leaf producing vastly different results from different creatures. Look at your own life: the same trial or blessing can produce gratitude in one person and bitterness in another. How does that diversity point to a wise Creator, and what does it demand from you when you face a situation that could go either way?

The Fifth Proof: Ja'far al-Sadiq's Egg Analogy
Ja'far al-Sadiq's egg analogy proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the transformation of an egg into a chick demonstrates the necessity of a Creator.

Core Idea
Even an egg demands a Maker.

Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq uses a vivid analogy from everyday observation to argue for the existence of God. He describes a sealed fortress that produces a living creature, then reveals it is actually an egg hatching. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy carefully
Notice the descriptive language of the fortress (external like silver, internal like gold) and the living creature that emerges.
Pay attention to the surprise revelation at the end.
2
Identify the key elements
The fortress represents the egg, and the animal represents the chick. Understand the metaphor.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does this process require a 'manager' and 'Maker'? Think about the transformation from non-living to living.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : سئل جعفر الصادق [ رضي الله عنه ] (۱) عن هذه المسألة أيضاً فقال : شاهدنا قلعة حصينة ملساء ، ظاهرها كالفضة المسبوكة ، وباطنها مملوء من الذهب المذاب [ والفضة المذابة ] (٢) ثم انشقت الجدران ، وخرج من القلعة حيوان سميع بصير. فلا بد من مدير يدبره ، وصانع يخلقه . وعنى بالقلعة : البيضة . وبالحيوان : الفرخ .
The fifth: Ja'far al-Sadiq (may God be pleased with him) (1) was asked about this matter as well, and he said: We saw a fortress: strong, smooth, its exterior like cast silver, its interior filled with molten gold and molten silver (2). Then the walls split, and from the fortress came out a living creature, hearing and seeing. So there must be a manager who manages it and a Maker who creates it. He meant by the fortress: the egg. And by the animal: the chick.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof, an argument by analogy from Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq. He describes a fortress with a silver-like exterior and molten gold and silver interior that splits open to produce a living, sentient animal. He then explains that this fortress is actually an egg, and the animal is a chick. The point is that such a perfectly constructed and sealed object producing a complex living being cannot happen without a purposeful manager and maker. This is analogous to the universe, which requires a Creator. The proof emphasizes the transformation from inanimate matter (egg components) to a living organism, highlighting the need for a designing agent.

الفرخ

?

?
The passage says there must be a manager who manages it. Do you truly live as if your life has a Manager? Or do you act as if you are in control of your own 'fortress'? What would change if you fully submitted to that management?

The Sixth Proof: Diversity of Voices (Imam Malik)
Sixth proof: Malik's argument from differences in sounds and languages.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Malik's argument from the diversity of voices, tones, and languages as evidence for the existence of a wise Creator.

Core Idea
Every unique voice reveals a unique Creator.

Imam Malik was once asked by Caliph Harun al-Rashid for a proof of God's existence. His response drew upon something we experience every day: the incredible diversity of human voices, musical tones, and languages. This simple yet profound observation, rooted in a Qur'anic verse, forms the sixth proof in our series. Let's explore how this diversity points to a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Understand the context
Note that the proof is presented in response to a question from Caliph Harun al-Rashid. Recognizing the setting helps appreciate the argument's simplicity.
3
Identify the three pieces of evidence
Malik points to differences in voices, variation in tones, and diversity of languages. List them and think about what makes each unique.
No two human voices are identical; musical tones vary infinitely; languages are vastly diverse.
4
Connect to the Qur'anic verse
Malik explicitly links his argument to Quran 30:22, which mentions diversity of languages and colors as signs. Consider how scripture reinforces empirical observation.
5
Reflect on why this proves a Creator
Ask yourself: can this diversity arise without a wise designer? The underlying uniformity of the vocal apparatus combined with infinite variation suggests intentional creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

All humans share the same basic physical apparatus for sound production, yet each individual voice is unique, and languages vary widely across communities. This variation without a predetermined cause suggests a wise Creator who intentionally creates diversity. The proof is linked to a Qur'anic verse (Surah Rum 30:22) that mentions diversity of languages and colors as signs of God.

The Seventh Proof: Poetic Argument from Design in Nature
Abu Nuwas' poetic proof from plant patterns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the poet Abu Nuwas used the intricate patterns in plants as a witness for God's oneness.

Core Idea
The beauty and order in nature bear witness that God has no partner.

The seventh proof presented by the author is a poetic reflection from the famous poet Abu Nuwas. Instead of a logical syllogism, this proof invites contemplation of the intricate designs found in plants. Abu Nuwas argues that these natural patterns are clear signs that point to a single Creator, challenging any notion of partners with God. In this lesson, we will unpack the verse and explore its theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic verse and translation
First, read the Arabic passage aloud if possible. Then read the literal translation to grasp the plain meaning.
Notice the poetic rhythm; this is a form of evidence, not just verse.
2
Identify key terms and their significance
Look at the key terms: تأمل (contemplate), نبات الأرض (plants), آثار (traces), المليك (the King), قضب الزبرجد (emerald stalks), شريك (partner). Each contributes to the proof.
3
Understand the argument: contemplation leads to witnessing
Abu Nuwas calls for contemplation of plants and then directs attention to the 'traces' of God's craftsmanship on emerald stalks. These traces are the patterns that serve as witnesses.
The word 'شاهدات' (they bear witness) is the conclusion.
4
Reflect on the aesthetic dimension
Consider why the poet uses 'emerald stalks' – a vivid image of green, life, and beauty. The argument appeals to both reason and aesthetic appreciation.
This shows that theological reflection can be inspired by beauty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The seventh: Abu Nuwas was asked about this matter, and he said: Contemplate the vegetation of the earth and look at the traces of what the King has made; on stalks of emerald they bear witness that God has no partner.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof in poetic form from the poet Abu Nuwas. He invites contemplation of plant life on earth, specifically pointing to the patterns and designs visible on 'stalks of emerald' (likely referring to the intricate patterns on leaves or stems). These natural designs serve as witnesses that God is one and has no partner. The proof is based on the beauty, order, and complexity in nature, which indicate a single Creator. The poetic form emphasizes reflection and aesthetic appreciation as a path to recognizing God's unity.

The Eighth Proof: The Bedouin's Analogy
Bedouin's analogy from dung and footprints.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Bedouin used everyday analogies to argue for the existence of the All-Powerful Creator.

Core Idea
Simple observations point to the greatest reality.

This lesson presents a powerful argument from analogy given by a Bedouin. Using examples like camel droppings indicating a camel, he shows that the magnificent sky and earth must point to their Creator. The proof is simple yet profound, relying on the universal principle that every effect has a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the rhythm and simplicity of the Bedouin's words.
The passage is short but rich in meaning.
2
Identify the analogies
The Bedouin gives three specific examples: camel droppings indicate a camel, horse dung indicates a donkey, and footprints indicate a traveler. Identify each and see how they follow the same pattern.
3
Understand the logical inference
The Bedouin then applies the same pattern to the sky and earth: they are effects that must have a cause. The cause is the All-Knowing, All-Powerful Being.
This is an argument from effect to cause.
4
Reflect on the strength
Reflect on why this analogy is powerful despite its simplicity. It appeals to common sense and clear observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : سئل أعرابي عن الدليل . فقال : البعرة تدل على البعير ، والروث على الحمير، وآثار الأقدام على المسير، فسماء ذات أبراج ، وأرض ذات فجاج ، أما تدل على العليم القدير؟
The eighth: A Bedouin was asked about the proof. He said: The camel dropping indicates the camel, and the horse dung indicates the donkey, and footprints indicate the traveler. So a sky with constellations, and an earth with mountain passes – do they not indicate the All-Knowing, All-Powerful?
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof, a straightforward argument from common sense given by an unnamed Bedouin. He uses three everyday examples: camel droppings indicate a camel, horse dung indicates a horse, and footprints indicate a traveler. By analogy, the existence of a sky filled with stars and constellations, and an earth with vast mountain passes and terrain, must indicate the existence of a Knower and Powerful Being who created them. The proof is based on the principle that every effect has a cause, and the more magnificent the effect, the more magnificent the cause.

الأرض ذات فجاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy is too crude or simplistic to be a valid proof.
Correction: The analogy is a powerful logical inference from effect to cause. Even simple observations can yield sound arguments for the existence of a Creator.

Checkpoint

A) A ship and its captain
B) Animal dung and its source
C) A book and its author
D) A house and its builder

Key Takeaway
The Bedouin's simple analogy demonstrates that even the most basic observations of cause and effect point to the existence of the All-Knowing, All-Powerful Creator of the heavens and the earth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Ninth Proof: Design in Medicine and Nature
Two physicians' arguments from opposite effects in medicine and nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two physicians used examples from nature and medicine to argue for the existence of a wise Creator.

Core Idea
Contraries in nature point to a single wise Designer.

This lesson presents the ninth proof from the text, which consists of two examples given by physicians. These examples use empirical observations of opposite effects in natural substances and creatures to argue that such purposeful design cannot be accidental. Let's examine how these simple observations lead to profound theological conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully, noting the two examples.
2
Understand the first example
The physician points to a purgative myrobalan that loosens and saliva that binds, both wet substances but opposite effects.
3
Understand the second example
Another physician points to the bee, which has poison in one part and healing in another.
4
Reflect on the argument
Both examples show that contrary properties in one substance or creature imply a wise assigner, not random chance.
This is a simple argument from design.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : قيل لطبيب : بم عرفت ربك ؟ فقال : بإهليج مخفف أطلق ولعاب ملين أمسك . وقال آخر : عرفته بحيوان صغير، وضع السم في أحد طرفيه ، والشفاء في طرفه الآخر . وعنى به النحل
The ninth: A physician was asked: 'By what did you know your Lord?' He said: 'By a purgative myrobalan that loosens, and by a softening saliva that binds.' And another said: 'I knew Him by a small animal that placed poison in one of its ends and a cure in the other end.' He meant thereby the bee.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof, consisting of two statements from physicians. The first physician argues from the opposite effects of natural substances: the myrobalan fruit acts as a laxative, while saliva acts as a binder (astringent). Despite being both wet substances, they produce opposite effects, indicating a wise designer who assigns properties. The second physician points to the bee, a small animal that carries both poison (in its sting) and healing (honey) in different parts of its body. This dual nature in one creature shows purposeful creation. Both examples highlight the presence of contraries and beneficial design in nature, pointing to a wise Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson presents the opening of Abu Hanifa's tenth proof — a story where he appears preoccupied with his belongings. Ja'far al-Sadiq's Egg Analogy
Ja'far al-Sadiq uses a parable of an egg to argue for a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ja'far al-Sadiq uses an analogy of an egg to argue for the existence of a creator.

Core Idea
An egg's transformation to a chick proves a wise maker.

This lesson presents a beautiful analogy from Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq. He describes an egg as a fortress with silver exterior and gold interior, then it splits open and a chick emerges. This vivid image leads to a clear rational argument for a creator, showing that such a remarkable transformation cannot happen without a director and maker.

Guided Path
1
Read the parable
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to absorb the imagery of the fortress and the chick.
Notice the contrast between the outer shell and inner contents.
2
Identify the elements
Identify the fortress (egg), the animal (chick), and the conclusion that there must be a director and maker.
3
Understand the analogy
Reflect on why the fortress analogy is used: just as a fortress cannot produce a living being from within without an external agent, the egg cannot produce a chick without a divine creator.
4
Apply the reasoning
Consider how this analogy extends to all complex beings in nature—they all point to a wise creator.
This is a rational argument, not just empirical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : سئل جعفر الصادق [ رضي الله عنه ] (۱) عن هذه المسألة أيضاً فقال : شاهدنا قلعة حصينة ملساء ، ظاهرها كالفضة المسبوكة ، وباطنها مملوء من الذهب المذاب [ والفضة المذابة ] (٢) ثم انشقت الجدران ، وخرج من القلعة حيوان سميع بصير. فلا بد من مدير يدبره ، وصانع يخلقه . وعنى بالقلعة : البيضة . وبالحيوان : الفرخ .
Fifth: Ja'far al-Sadiq, may Allah be pleased with him, was asked about this matter again and said: We observed a strong, smooth fortress, its exterior like cast silver, its interior filled with molten gold and molten silver, then the walls split open and from the fortress emerged a hearing, seeing animal. Then there must be a director who manages it and a maker who creates it. He meant by the fortress: the egg, and by the animal: the chick.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy for the existence of a creator. Ja'far al-Sadiq describes an egg as a fortress with a hard shell and interior contents, then it spontaneously splits and a chick emerges. He argues that such a transformation cannot happen without a director and a creator. The analogy illustrates that just as a fortress cannot produce an animal from within without an external agent, so the egg cannot produce a chick without a divine creator. The passage concludes by clarifying the symbolism: the fortress is the egg and the animal is the chick.

Key Terms
قلعة (fortress)
بيضة (egg)
فرخ (chick)
مدير (director)
صانع (maker)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this is a purely empirical argument based on observation of eggs.
Correction: It is actually a rational analogy about causality: the egg's transformation requires an external director and maker, not just natural processes.

Checkpoint

A) The world
B) The egg
C) The chick
D) The creator

Key Takeaway
The egg's transformation into a chick, like a fortress producing a living animal, necessitates a divine creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Sixth Proof: Malik's Argument from Diversity
Malik argues from differences in voices, melodies, and languages to a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Malik used the observable diversity in voices, melodies, and languages to argue for the existence of a wise creator.

Core Idea
Diversity in creation points to a single wise Creator.

Malik ibn Anas, the founder of the Maliki school, was asked by Caliph Harun al-Rashid about the proof for God's existence. His response was elegantly simple: look at the differences in human voices, the variety of melodies, and the multiplicity of languages. These phenomena cannot be random; they must be the work of a purposeful Creator. In this lesson, we examine this argument and its Quranic foundation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Malik was responding to a direct question from Caliph Harun al-Rashid. This shows the practical need for clear arguments for God's existence.
The caliph's question reflects the intellectual climate of the time.
2
Identify the three elements of diversity
Notice the three specific phenomena Malik points to: differences in voices (اختلاف الأصوات), modulation of melodies (تردد النغمات), and diversity of languages (تفاوت اللغات). Each is an observable fact.
3
Recognize the Quranic basis
Malik directly cites Quran 30:22, which mentions diversity of languages and colors as signs of Allah. This shows his argument is rooted in revelation.
The verse also includes 'colors', which Malik extends to voices and melodies in his reasoning.
4
Reflect on the logical inference
Ask yourself: why does this diversity imply a creator? The key is that such variety cannot arise from mere chance or human design; it requires a wise, purposeful agent.
This is an argument from design, focusing on the harmony and order underlying apparent randomness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : سأل هارون الرشيد مالكا عن ذلك ، فاستدل باختلاف الأصوات ، وتردد النغمات ، وتفاوت اللغات ، وهو مأخوذ من القرآن في قوله تعالى : ( واختلاف السنتكم وألوانكم ) (٣)
Sixth: Harun al-Rashid asked Malik about that, so he argued by the difference of voices, the modulation of melodies, and the diversity of languages, and this is taken from the Quran in His statement: 'And the diversity of your languages and colors.'
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the observable variety in human traits. Malik argues that the differences in voices, the variation in musical notes, and the multiplicity of languages cannot arise from chance or human design; they must be the work of a wise creator. He supports this by citing Quran 30:22, which mentions diversity of languages and colors as signs of Allah.

Key Terms
اختلاف الأصوات (difference of voices)
النغمات (melodies)
اللغات (languages)
القرآن (Quran)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this is a trivial observation that does not constitute a serious argument.
Correction: In reality, it is a profound theological argument from order and design, recognized by classical scholars and grounded in the Quran.

Checkpoint

B) 2:255
C) 112:1

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The beauty and design of plants bear witness to the oneness of the Creator.

Abu Nuwas was asked about a theological matter and responded with poetry. His poem invites us to reflect on the vegetation of the earth, pointing to the traces of the Creator's work. This lesson explores how this poetic argument serves as a proof for tawhid.

The argument is that such design cannot be from multiple sources.
4

The Bedouin's Proof from Traces
A Bedouin argues from animal droppings and footprints to the Creator of the cosmos.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Bedouin's analogy from everyday traces leads to a powerful proof for the existence of Allah, and why this argument resonates across cultures.

Core Idea
Tracks in the sand point to a traveler; the cosmos points to its Creator.

This lesson presents one of the most famous and intuitive arguments for God's existence: the Bedouin's analogy. Often dismissed as simplistic, this argument actually captures the universal human intuition that every effect must have a cause. As you read the short passage, notice how the Bedouin moves from familiar traces to the grandest of signs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the rhythm of the Bedouin's words sink in.
Pay attention to the rhetorical question at the end.
2
Identify the three examples of traces
The Bedouin gives three examples: camel droppings, donkey dung, and footprints. For each, identify what it indicates (camel, donkey, journey).
These are all effects pointing to a specific cause.
3
Notice the rhetorical question
After the examples, the Bedouin asks: 'So a sky with constellations and an earth with ravines — do they not indicate the All-Knowing, All-Powerful?' Consider why this question is powerful.
It expects a 'yes' from anyone who accepts the earlier logic.
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
The argument relies on the principle that every effect has a cause. The cosmos is an effect requiring a cause worthy of its grandeur.
This is known as the argument from traces (istidlāl bi'l-āthār).
5
Connect to the nature of the Creator
The Creator is described as All-Knowing and All-Powerful. This matches the intricate design of the sky and earth.
The attributes are derived from the traces themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : سئل أعرابي عن الدليل . فقال : البعرة تدل على البعير ، والروث على الحمير، وآثار الأقدام على المسير، فسماء ذات أبراج ، وأرض ذات فجاج ، أما تدل على العليم القدير؟
Eighth: A Bedouin was asked about the proof. He said: The camel dropping indicates the camel, the dung indicates the donkey, and the footprints indicate the journey. So a sky with constellations and an earth with ravines — do they not indicate the All-Knowing, All-Powerful?
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a simple yet powerful analogy. The Bedouin uses everyday examples: camel droppings imply a camel, donkey dung implies a donkey, footprints imply a traveler. By analogy, the heavens with their constellations and the earth with its vast ravines must indicate the existence of an All-Knowing, All-Powerful Creator. The argument is based on the principle that every effect has a cause, and the grand scale of the universe demands a grand Cause.

Key Terms
بعرة
روث
آثار الأقدام
سماء ذات أبراج
أرض ذات فجاج
العليم القدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this is a simplistic analogy that lacks depth.
Correction: In reality, it is a powerful expression of the universal causal principle that every effect has a cause, and it resonates across cultures.

Checkpoint
What does the Bedouin compare the sky and earth to in his analogy?
A) Camel droppings and footprints
B) A camel and a donkey
C) A journey and a traveler
D) Constellations and ravines

Key Takeaway
The Bedouin's proof shows that the same logic we use to infer causes from everyday effects leads us to infer the existence of a powerful Creator from the grand design of the universe.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the sky and earth, do you genuinely see them as signs pointing to the All-Knowing and All-Powerful, or have you become numb to their testimony? What would it take for you to see the world through the Bedouin's eyes today?
?
If a Bedouin with no formal education could articulate this proof so clearly, what excuse do you have for ignoring the signs around you? Will you commit to stopping right now and reflecting on one sign until your heart acknowledges its Creator?
?
The Bedouin's proof is based on the simple logic of cause and effect. Do you live your life as if the universe has a purpose and a Creator, or do you act as if it all happened by chance? How would your daily decisions change if you truly believed every constellation and ravine is a direct message from God to you?

Abu Hanifa's Ship Analogy: Self-Evident Need for a Creator
Abu Hanifa uses the impossibility of a self-moving ship to prove a Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Hanifa used a simple analogy to demonstrate that the need of contingent things for a Creator is self-evident.

Core Idea
If a ship cannot move itself, how can the universe?

In this passage, Abu Hanifa employs a clever Socratic dialogue to lead his listeners to affirm the existence of a Creator. By describing an absurd scenario of self-loading ships, he provokes them to reject it as impossible without a director, then applies the same logic to the world. The result is a powerful reminder that the need for a Creator is obvious to sound reason.

Guided Path
1
Follow the dialogue
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Abu Hanifa sets up a seemingly unrelated story to trap his listeners into admitting a principle.
2
Identify the listeners' objection
When he describes the ships moving by themselves, the listeners call him mad. This is the key turning point.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's rebuttal
He uses their own reasoning against them: if a ship needs a director, the world needs one even more.
4
Understand the self-evident nature
The passage concludes that the need for a Creator is self-evident (بديهي). Reflect on why this is considered independent of proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : سئل أبو حنيفة عن الدليل مرة أخرى فقال : اصبروا فإن قلبي مشغول ببعض المهمات ، وإذا فرغت منه أخبرتكم (4) بذلك [ الدليل ] (*) فقالوا : وما ذلك المهم ؟ فقال : إن أمتعتي من ذلك الجانب من دجلة ، وإن بعض السفن تذهب بنفسها إلى ذلك الجانب وتلك الأمتعة تقع في تلك السفينة من تلقاء ذواتها ، وتمتلىء السفينة من تلك الأمتعة [ وتعود إلى هذا الجانب ، وتخرج الأمتعة من تلقاء نفسها ، وتقع على الأرض ، وإن تلك السفينة ] (١) تعود مرة أخرى إلى ذلك الجانب . فقلبي متعلق . بهذا [ المعنى ] (٢) المهم فاصبروا إلى أن يتم . فقالوا يا أبا حنيفة . هذا الكلام لا يقوله إلا معتوه . قال : ولم ؟ قالوا : لأن حدوث هذه الأحوال ، وظهور هذه الأفعال لا يعقل إلا بحافظ يحفظها ، وفاعل يدبرها . فكيف يعقل حدوثها من غير فاعل ؟ فقال : أبو حنيفة : فالآن أقررتم بوجود الإله . لأن أحوال هذا العالم ليست أقل درجة من أحوال هذه السفينة. فإذا كان صريح العقل شاهد بأن هذه الأحوال لا تتم إلا بمدير ومقدر ، فأولى أن لا تتم أحوال هذا العالم إلا بمدير ومقدر ؟ واعلم أن مقصود أبي حنيفة من هذا الكلام : التنبيه على أن العلم بافتقار الحادث [ إلى المحدث علم بديهي ] ضروري غني عن الدليل (۴)
Tenth: Abu Hanifa was asked about the proof another time and said: Be patient, for my heart is occupied with some important matters, and when I finish I will tell you that proof. They said: And what is that important matter? He said: My goods are on that side of the Tigris, and some ships go by themselves to that side, and those goods fall into that ship of their own accord, and the ship fills with those goods, and it returns to this side, and the goods come out by themselves, and fall on the ground, and that ship returns again to that side. My heart is attached to this important matter, so be patient until it is done. They said: O Abu Hanifa, no one says this except a madman. He said: Why? They said: Because the occurrence of these states and the appearance of these actions cannot be conceived except by a preserver who preserves them and an agent who manages them. So how can their occurrence without an agent be conceived? Then Abu Hanifa said: Now you have affirmed the existence of Allah. For the states of this world are not less in degree than the states of this ship. So if the clear intellect testifies that these states cannot be completed except by a director and determiner, then it is more fitting that the states of this world cannot be completed except by a director and determiner. And know that the purpose of Abu Hanifa in this speech is to indicate that knowledge of the contingency's need for the creator is self-evident, necessary, and independent of proof.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a clever argument by Abu Hanifa using the Socratic method. He describes an absurd scenario: ships moving by themselves and loading/unloading cargo spontaneously. His listeners immediately reject this as madness, arguing that such events require a preserver and agent. Abu Hanifa then turns the tables: if they admit that a simple ship cannot function without a director, then the entire universe, far more complex, must also have a Director. The conclusion is that the need of a contingent thing for a creator is self-evident (بديهي) and does not require elaborate proof.

Key Terms
سفينة (ship)
دجلة (Tigris)
أمتعة (goods)
مدير ومقدر (director and determiner)
علم بديهي (self-evident knowledge)
حادث (contingent)
محدث (creator)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Abu Hanifa was actually worried about his goods.
Correction: It was a pedagogical ruse to lead his listeners to the conclusion.

Checkpoint
What does Abu Hanifa's listeners say about his scenario of self-moving ships?
A) It is possible with time
B) Only a madman would say that
C) It is a miracle
D) It is a logical proof

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's ship analogy demonstrates that the need for a Creator is so obvious that even denying it leads to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Abu Hanifa used a ship to jolt his listeners. What 'ship' in your life—some obvious order or blessing—have you overlooked as evidence of Allah? Will you acknowledge it now?

Ja'far ibn Muhammad's Argument from Self-Existence
Ja'far ibn Muhammad argues that his own existence proves a Creator distinct from himself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ja'far ibn Muhammad proves the existence of God by reflecting on the contingency of his own existence.

Core Idea
My own existence is the clearest proof of a Creator distinct from me.

In this lesson, we examine a powerful argument attributed to Ja'far ibn Muhammad that uses self-awareness as a starting point. The argument is elegantly simple: your existence had a beginning, so it requires a cause. But you cannot be that cause yourself. Therefore, the cause must be God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the direct self-referential style.
The argument starts from personal experience, not external cosmology.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Ja'far eliminates two scenarios: that he created himself while existing or while non-existing. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the impossibility
Why can't you create yourself? If you already exist, you don't need a creator. If you don't exist, you cannot act. Grasp this logical dilemma.
This is the core of the argument – a tautology and a contradiction.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since you are not the cause of your own existence, the cause must be something other than you, and that something must be an existent being. This is the Creator.
The conclusion is that the maker is an existent other than yourself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحادي عشر : سئل جعفر بن محمد مرة أخرى عن الدليل فقال : أقوى الدلائل على وجوده : وجودي [ وذلك لأن وجودي ] (1) حدث بعد أن لم يكن فله فاعل . ويمتنع أن يقال : فاعل وجودي أنا . لأنه لا يخلو إما أن يقال : أنا أحدثت نفسي ، حال ما كنت موجودا ، أو حال ما كنت معدوما . فإن أحدثت نفسي حال ما كنت موجودا فالموجود أي حاجة به إلى الموجد ؟ وإن أحدثت نفسي حال ما كنت معدوما [ فالمعدوم ] كيف يكون موجدا للموجود ؟ فدل هذا على أن الصانع الفاعل لوجودي : موجود غير غيري .
Eleventh: Ja'far ibn Muhammad was asked about the proof another time and said: The strongest evidence for His existence is my existence. For my existence came into being after it was not, so it has a maker. And it is impossible to say: I am the maker of my existence. Because it is not free from being said: I brought myself into existence either while I was existing or while I was non-existent. If I brought myself into existence while I was existing, then the existent has no need for an originator. And if I brought myself into existence while I was non-existent, then how can the non-existent be an originator for the existent? So this indicates that the maker and agent of my existence is an existent other than myself.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a concise logical proof for the existence of God from personal contingency. Ja'far ibn Muhammad argues that his own existence, which began after not existing, must have a cause. He then eliminates the possibility that he himself is that cause through a disjunctive syllogism: either he created himself while he existed (which is redundant) or while he did not exist (which is impossible). Therefore, the cause must be an external, self-sufficient being — God. This argument is a classic formulation of the contingency argument (دليل الحدوث) applied to oneself.

Key Terms
وجودي (my existence)
فاعل (maker)
أحدثت نفسي (I brought myself into existence)
موجود (existent)
معدوم (non-existent)
صانع فاعل (maker agent)
غير غيري (other than myself)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is the same as Descartes' cogito ergo sum.
Correction: Ja'far's argument is from temporal origination (ḥudūth), not from the certainty of self-existence. It assumes the self's contingency, not its indubitability.

Checkpoint
According to Ja'far ibn Muhammad, what is the strongest evidence for God's existence?
A) The creation of the heavens
B) His own existence
C) The prophets
D) The Quran

Key Takeaway
Ja'far ibn Muhammad proves God's existence by showing that his own contingent existence cannot be self-caused, necessitating an external Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own existence is the clearest proof of a Creator, why do you still live as if you are self-sufficient and independent?
?
When you look in the mirror, do you see a self-made person or a living sign of the One who brought you from non-existence?
?
How would your daily actions change if you genuinely remembered that every moment of your existence is sustained by Another, not by yourself?

Abu Hanifa's Ship Analogy for the Necessity of a Creator
Abu Hanifa presents a ship analogy to argue for a Creator and asserts the intuitive nature of this knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Hanifa uses a simple analogy of a ship to argue that the world requires a Creator and why this knowledge is considered self-evident.

Core Idea
If a ship needs a manager, the world needs a Creator – and this is intuitively known.

This passage from a classical work recounts a famous story of Imam Abu Hanifa using a vivid analogy to demonstrate the necessity of a Creator. As you read, notice how he turns the listeners' objection into an admission of God's existence. The commentary explains that for Abu Hanifa, this truth is so obvious it needs no proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the story and note the scenario
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Pay attention to the ship's self-operating description Abu Hanifa presents.
The listeners initially call him mad for such a description.
2
Identify the logical structure
Notice how the listeners' objection (that the ship needs a guardian) becomes the very proof Abu Hanifa uses. They admit it's impossible without an agent; he applies the same logic to the world.
This is an argument from analogy.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author states that Abu Hanifa's goal is to show that the need of the originated for an originator is self-evident knowledge (badihi). Consider why this might be considered intuitive.
No elaborate proof is needed according to this view.
4
Connect to broader theology
Think about how this argument fits into the classical Islamic proofs for God's existence. It is a simple, powerful demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعض السفن تذهب بنفسها إلى ذلك الجانب وتلك الأمتعة تقع في تلك السفينة من تلقاء ذواتها، وتمتلىء السفينة من تلك الأمتعة وتعود إلى هذا الجانب، وتخرج الأمتعة من تلقاء نفسها، وتقع على الأرض، وإن تلك السفينة تعود مرة أخرى إلى ذلك الجانب. فقلبي متعلق بهذا [المعنى] المهم فاصبروا إلى أن يتم. فقالوا يا أبا حنيفة هذا الكلام لا يقوله إلا معتوه. قال: ولم؟ قالوا: لأن حدوث هذه الأحوال، وظهور هذه الأفعال لا يعقل إلا بحافظ يحفظها، وفاعل يدبرها. فكيف يعقل حدوثها من غير فاعل؟ فقال أبو حنيفة: فالآن أقررتم بوجود الإله. لأن أحوال هذا العالم ليست أقل درجة من أحوال هذه السفينة. فإذا كان صريح العقل شاهد بأن هذه الأحوال لا تتم إلا بمدير ومقدر، فأولى أن لا تتم أحوال هذا العالم إلا بمدير ومقدر؟ واعلم أن مقصود أبي حنيفة من هذا الكلام: التنبيه على أن العلم بافتقار الحادث [إلى المحدث علم بديهي] ضروري غني عن الدليل.
Some ships go by themselves to that side, and those loads fall into that ship by themselves, and the ship fills with those loads and returns to this side, and the loads come out by themselves and fall to the ground, and that ship returns again to that side. My heart is attached to this important meaning, so be patient until it is complete. They said: O Abu Hanifa, no one says such speech but a madman. He said: Why? They said: Because the occurrence of these states and the appearance of these actions are not conceivable except by a preserver who preserves them and an agent who manages them. So how can their occurrence be conceived without an agent? Abu Hanifa said: Now you have acknowledged the existence of the deity (al-ilah). For the states of this world are not lesser in degree than the states of this ship. If the clear intellect testifies that these states are not accomplished except by a manager and a determiner, then it is more fitting that the states of this world are not accomplished except by a manager and a determiner. And know that Abu Hanifa's intent in this speech is to indicate that knowledge of the need of the originated (al-hadith) for the originator (al-muhdith) is a self-evident (badihi) necessary (daruri) knowledge, independent of proof.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a narrative proof for God's existence. It recounts a story in which Abu Hanifa describes a ship that seemingly operates without a crew. The listeners object that such events require a guardian and an agent. Abu Hanifa then turns the objection into an acknowledgment: if they concede that the ship needs a director, then the world, which is far more complex, must also have a director. The author then clarifies that Abu Hanifa's point is that the need of every originated thing for an originator is intuitive and needs no elaborate proof.

Key Terms
مدير
مقدر
فاعل
حادث
محدث
علم بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ship analogy is merely a hypothetical story without logical force.
Correction: It is a logical argument that uses the principle of analogy: if the world is more complex than a ship, it requires at least as much management, which points to a Creator.

Checkpoint
What did Abu Hanifa claim the listeners had implicitly acknowledged when they objected that the ship's actions require a guardian?
A) The existence of a ship captain
B) The existence of a Creator for the world
C) The unreliability of sense perception
D) The need for empirical evidence

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's ship analogy shows that the need of everything originated for an originator is so evident that even acknowledging a need for a manager in a simple ship leads to acknowledging a Creator for the universe.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that the need for a Creator is self-evident. If you truly believe this, why is tawheed not the first thing on your mind every moment? Commit to one action today that reminds you of your absolute dependence on Allah.
?
Abu Hanifa's audience immediately rejected absurdity. What absurdities in your own life do you overlook because you don't want to face the reality of a Creator? Will you confront that tonight?
?
The proof is independent of proof. Has your heart truly accepted this, or do you still seek more evidence? Ask Allah for yaqin (certainty) right now, and make a sincere du'a that He makes this truth settle in your soul.

Ja'far al-Sadiq's Argument from Self-Existence
Ja'far al-Sadiq argues that one's own contingent existence proves a Creator outside oneself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ja'far al-Sadiq uses the impossibility of self-creation to prove the existence of a Creator distinct from oneself.

Core Idea
Your own existence is the strongest proof for God.

In this passage, Ja'far al-Sadiq responds to a question about the proof for God's existence. He points directly to one's own existence as the most compelling evidence. By examining the impossibility of being one's own creator, he shows that a Maker must exist outside of oneself.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument.
Notice the concise style of Ja'far al-Sadiq.
2
Identify the key disjunction
Ja'far presents two alternatives for self-creation: either while existing or while non-existent. Identify these and see why both are impossible.
This is a disjunctive syllogism.
3
Understand the conclusion
The only remaining possibility is that the creator is another being distinct from oneself. Reflect on why this follows logically.
4
Reflect on the strength of the argument
Consider why Ja'far calls this the strongest proof. It is based on immediate personal experience and logical necessity.
The argument does not rely on complex metaphysical assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سئل جعفر بن محمد مرة أخرى عن الدليل فقال: أقوى الدلائل على وجوده: وجودي وذلك لأن وجودي حدث بعد أن لم يكن فله فاعل. ويمتنع أن يقال: فاعل وجودي أنا. لأنه لا يخلو إما أن يقال: أنا أحدثت نفسي، حال ما كنت موجودا، أو حال ما كنت معدوما. فإن أحدثت نفسي حال ما كنت موجودا فالموجود أي حاجة به إلى الموجد؟ وإن أحدثت نفسي حال ما كنت معدوما فالمعدوم كيف يكون موجدا للموجود؟ فدل هذا على أن الصانع الفاعل لوجودي: موجود غير غيري.
Ja'far ibn Muhammad was asked another time about the proof, and he said: The strongest of proofs for His existence is my existence, because my existence occurred after it was not, so it has an agent. It is impossible to say that I am the agent of my existence. Because inevitably either it is said that I originated myself while I was existing, or while I was non-existent. If I originated myself while I was existing, then the existent—what need does it have for the originator? And if I originated myself while I was non-existent, then how can the non-existent be an originator of the existent? This indicates that the Maker, the agent of my existence, is an existent other than myself.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a concise proof from personal contingency. Ja'far al-Sadiq begins by stating that one's own existence is the strongest proof for God. He then argues that the self cannot be its own creator, considering two impossible scenarios: if one created oneself while already existing, creation is redundant; if one created oneself while non-existent, a non-existent cannot create. Therefore, the creator must be another being distinct from oneself.

Key Terms
فاعل
موجد
موجود
معدوم
صانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument relies on an infinite regress of creators.
Correction: The argument does not regress; it uses a disjunctive syllogism to eliminate self-creation, leading directly to an external creator.

Checkpoint

B) I created myself either while existing or while non-existent

D) I created myself either directly or indirectly

Key Takeaway
My own existence, being contingent, necessitates an external Creator who is unlike me.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If you could not have created yourself, then your existence is entirely a gift. Are you living as if your life is a loan from Allah? What would change if you truly believed that?

Ali's Argument from the Failure of Intentions
Ali offers a proof from the failure of intentions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the common experience of effort not matching results points to a higher power that governs all affairs.

Core Idea
The gap between what we intend and what happens reveals a greater Planner.

Ali ibn Abi Talib was once asked for a proof of God's existence. His answer was surprisingly simple: look at your own intentions. In this passage, he explains that the frequent failure of our best efforts and the occasional success without effort shows that there is a power beyond us that directs outcomes. This argument, grounded in everyday experience, remains one of the most accessible proofs in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the rhythm of Ali's reasoning: he begins with a terse claim, then unpacks it.
The passage is short but dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two observations
Notice that Ali describes two situations: striving without success, and success without striving. These are the raw data of the argument.
3
Follow the conditional logic
Ali then presents a conditional: if human effort were the sole cause, then effort would always lead to success. Since this is not the case, the conditional fails, and we infer a higher cause.
This is a classic argument from contingency or design.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that management of the world and its events depends on a wise, powerful Being who supersedes human power. Ask yourself: does this argument disprove human effort entirely?
No, it only shows effort is not the ultimate determinant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سئل علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه عن الدليل فقال: الدليل عليه نقص العزائم، وفسخ الهمم، وتقرير هذا الدليل: هو أن الإنسان يسعى في تحصيل شيء من المطالب على أقصى الوجوه ويتعذر عليه ذلك وقد لا يسعى في تحصيله البتة فيحصل. فلو كان حصول هذه الأحوال بسعيه، لوجب أن يحصل ما سعى في تحصيله، وأن يمتنع ما سعى في امتناعه، ولما كان الأمر بالعكس من ذلك. علمنا: أن تدبير أحوال هذا العالم، وتقدير حوادثه يتعلق بحكمة موجود قهر بقدرته قدر العباد.
Ali ibn Abi Talib, may God be pleased with him, was asked about the proof, and he said: The proof of Him is the failure of resolves and the dissolution of intentions. The exposition of this proof is that a man strives to obtain something of his desires in the utmost ways, yet it becomes impossible for him, while sometimes he does not strive for its attainment at all, yet it is obtained. If the attainment of these states were by his effort, then what he strove to attain would necessarily be attained, and what he strove to prevent would necessarily be prevented. But since the matter is the opposite of that, we know that the management of the affairs of this world and the determination of its events are dependent upon a wisdom of an existent who has subdued by His power the power of servants.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the mismatch between human effort and results. Ali begins by stating that the proof is the failure of intentions. He explains that often a person tries very hard to achieve something but fails, while sometimes he achieves something without any effort. If human effort were the sole cause, then effort would always lead to success and lack of effort to failure. Since this is not the case, there must be a higher power that overrides human intentions and determines outcomes.

Key Terms
نقص العزائم
فسخ الهمم
سعي
تدبير
تقدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument denies human free will or effort entirely.
Correction: The argument only shows that human effort is not the ultimate determinant. Effort is still required, but results depend on God's will.

Checkpoint
According to Ali's argument, what would necessarily happen if outcomes were entirely dependent on human effort?
A) Everything would be chaotic
B) What one strives for would be achieved and what one strives to avoid would be avoided
C) People would stop striving
D) Only the intelligent would succeed

Key Takeaway
The frequent mismatch between human effort and actual outcomes points to a divine power that ultimately governs all affairs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you poured all your energy into something and it failed. How did you feel? Did that failure reveal to you that your ultimate reliance was on yourself rather than on Allah? What would it mean for your daily life to truly embrace that final outcomes are in His hands? Commit to one specific area where you will consciously surrender the result to Allah after doing your part.
?
Consider something you obtained without much effort—a blessing you didn't earn. Did you recognize it as a sign of a Giver beyond your striving, or did you take it for granted? How would your gratitude change if you saw every effortless success as a direct pointer to 'the wisdom of an existent who subdues the power of servants'? What is one blessing you will thank Allah for today specifically because you did not work for it?
?
This argument forces you to confront the gap between your plans and reality. Are you living as if your intentions are the final cause of events? Identify a current goal where you are anxiously trying to control every outcome. What would it look like to 'plan, but trust the Planner'? Write a short prayer acknowledging that God's management overrides your management, and commit to a concrete act of tawakkul (reliance) this week.

Umar's Argument from the Diversity of Faces
Umar compares the chessboard to the human face, arguing the infinite variety of faces proves a powerful Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogical argument from the diversity of human faces that proves the existence of a powerful and wise Creator.

Core Idea
Infinite variety from few fixed features points to a Creator of complete power.

In this passage, Umar ibn al-Khattab presents a profound argument for the existence of God based on the diversity of human faces. He draws an analogy with chess, noting that despite the small board and limited pieces, an infinite number of games are possible. He then points out that the human face is even smaller and its features are fixed, yet the variety of faces is endless. This striking contrast leads to a powerful conclusion about the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a feel for Umar's argument. Pay attention to the analogy he uses.
The language is straightforward but the argument is deep.
2
Identify the analogy
Notice how Umar compares the chessboard to the human face. List the similarities and differences he mentions.
Chess pieces can move; facial features are fixed.
3
Understand the conclusion
Umar concludes that the infinite variety of faces despite these limitations indicates the Creator's complete power. Reflect on why this follows.
The conclusion is not just about faces but about all diversity in creation.
4
Explore the expansion
Umar extends the argument to all human attributes—character, handwriting, etc. Consider how this strengthens the case.
5
Take the checkpoint
Answer the quiz question to test your understanding of the key contrast Umar makes.
The question focuses on the difference between chess and the face.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سئل عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه عن الدليل فقال: إن الناس يبالغون في تعظيم الأعاجم حيث استخرجوا علم الشطرنج فإنهم وضعوا طريقا عجيبا، يظهر في الرقعة المختصرة أنواعا من اللعب والمماسات غير متناهية. ثم قال: وإن رقعة وجه الإنسان أصغر من رقعة الشطرنج، وإن تلك المماسات الموضوعة في بيوت الشطرنج قد تنتقل من بيت إلى بيت، وأما الأعضاء الموضوعة في رقعة وجه الإنسان فإنها لا تنتقل عن أمكنتها. ثم مع هذه الأحوال، فإنك لا ترى إنسانين في المشرق، والمغرب يتشابهان في صورة الوجه البتة. وهذا يدل على كمال قدرة هذا الخالق المصور حيث أظهر الأنواع التي لا نهاية لها من التفاوت، في هذه الأجسام القليلة الموضوعة في هذه الرقعة الصغيرة. واعلم أنك إذا وقفت على هذه الدقيقة علمت أنه لا يوجد إنسانان تتشابه صورتاهما البتة، ولا يوجد إنسانان تتشابه أحوالهما حتى أن كل واحد لا بد وأن يخالف صاحبه في الطبع والخلق والمشي وفي كسور العمامة. وفي الخط، والكتابة، والحظ من السعادة والشقاوة. وهذا باب واسع وبحر لا ساحل له.
Umar ibn al-Khattab, may God be pleased with him, was asked about the proof, and he said: People exaggerate in magnifying the non-Arabs because they extracted the science of chess, for they devised a wondrous way: in the small board there appear types of plays and movements without end. Then he said: The board of the human face is smaller than the chessboard, and those movements placed in the squares of the chessboard can move from square to square, but the organs placed in the board of the human face do not move from their places. Then despite these conditions, you do not see two people in the East and the West whose face-pictures completely resemble each other. This indicates the complete power of this Creator, the Form-giver, as He has manifested infinite types of variance in these few bodies placed on this small board. And know that if you grasp this subtlety, you will know that no two people have completely resembling forms, nor do any two people have resembling states—even to the point that each one must differ from his companion in temperament, character, gait, the folds of the turban, handwriting, writing, and share of happiness and misery. This is a wide door and a sea without shore.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is an argument from design and diversity. Umar begins by marveling at the infinite variety of chess moves despite a small board. He then compares it to the human face, which has even fewer parts without the ability to move. Yet the diversity of human faces is infinite — no two people are exactly alike. The author adds that this diversity extends to all aspects of a person's life and character. This immense variety within such limited parameters points to a Creator of complete power and wisdom.

Key Terms
رقعة الشطرنج
رقعة وجه الإنسان
خالق
مصور
تفاوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this is merely a poetic observation about the uniqueness of faces.
Correction: It is actually a structured analogical argument: from few fixed parts producing infinite variations, it logically concludes a powerful Creator.

Checkpoint
What is the key contrast Umar makes between the chessboard and the human face?
A) The chessboard is larger than the face
B) Chess pieces can move while facial features cannot
C) Chess is invented by humans while the face is divine
D) The chessboard has more pieces than the face has features

Key Takeaway
Umar's argument shows that the infinite diversity of human faces and attributes from limited, fixed parts points unmistakably to a Creator of complete power and wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have never met a person with the exact same face, temperament, or handwriting as you. Does this uniqueness make you feel more accountable to the One who created you with such precision? How does knowing that your entire being is a singular creation affect your relationship with Allah today?
?
Umar says that no two people share the same 'share of happiness and misery.' If your life's happiness and hardship are uniquely designed for you, are you content with what Allah has written? Is there a specific trial or blessing you have been resisting or ungrateful for, thinking it was random?
?
Consider the 'folds of your turban'—the small, personal details of your life that no one else has. Are you living as if your unique traits are a gift from the Form-giver, or do you ignore them as mundane? Will you commit today to thank Allah for at least one specific detail that makes you distinct from everyone else?

The Minister's Palace Analogy: Proving God's Existence
A minister proves God's existence by analogy: a palace cannot appear without a builder.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the minister uses a clever analogy to prove that the universe requires a Creator, and why the king accepts this reasoning.

Core Idea
If a palace can't appear without a builder, the universe can't exist without God.

This story from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a powerful argument for God's existence using a simple analogy. A wise minister stages a scenario to jolt his king out of doubt. By first making the king reject the idea that a palace could arise spontaneously, the minister then applies the same logic to the entire cosmos. This lesson unpacks the story and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the story carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the narrative flow. Notice the characters and the sequence of events.
Pay attention to the king's initial doubt and his reaction.
2
Identify the minister's stratagem
The minister creates a built-up area and then claims it appeared by itself. Why does he do this? What does he hope to achieve?
The minister is setting a trap for the king's own reasoning.
3
Analyze the analogy
The minister argues that if a palace cannot arise without a builder, then the vast universe cannot exist without a Creator. Compare the scale of the two: a small palace vs. the entire cosmos.
This is an argument from design, using a fortuitous palace as a microcosm.
4
Reflect on the king's response
The king becomes angry at first but then realizes the logic and returns to true religion. What does this tell us about the power of rational argument in faith?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع عشر : كان بعض الملوك قد وقع في قلبه شك في وجود الله تعالى ، وكان له وزير عاقل ، وكانت العادة جارية بأن ذلك الوزير كان يتخذ لذلك الملك ضيافة في كل سنة مرة واحدة فأمر الوزير في بعض المفاوز الخالية بوضع الزرع والضرع فيها وباجراء المياه الجارية وبناء البساتين الطيبة ، ووضع القصور العالية . ثم حضر السلطان في تلك المواضع فتعجب السلطان ، وقال للوزير : كيف عمرت هذه المواضع ؟ فقال الوزير: أطال الله بقاء السلطان إني ما عمرتها ، ولكنها كانت خراباً قفراً الى هذه الأيام القريبة ، ثم إنا لما عدنا وجدنا هذه الأشياء حدثت من تلقاء نفسها من غير بان ولا مصلح ، فاشتد غضب السلطان ، وقال : كيف يليق بمثلك أن يهزأ بي ؟ فقال : أطال الله بقاء السلطان ، إذا كان حدوث هذا القدر من العمارة بلا معمار ولا مصلح ممتنعاً ، فحدوث العالم الأعلى والأسفل مع كثرة ما فيها من العجائب ، والغرائب بلا موجود ولا مدبر أولى بالامتناع . فانتبه السلطان من هذا الكلام ، وعاد إلى الدين الحق
The seventeenth: There was a king who had a doubt in his heart about the existence of God, and he had a wise minister. The custom was that the minister would prepare a feast for the king once every year. The minister ordered that in some empty wilderness, crops and livestock be placed, flowing waters be channeled, pleasant gardens be built, and high palaces be erected. Then the king attended those places and was amazed. He said to the minister: How did you build these places? The minister said: May God prolong the king's life, I did not build them; rather they were barren desert until recently. Then when we returned, we found these things came into existence by themselves without any builder or organizer. The king became very angry and said: How is it fitting for someone like you to mock me? He said: May God prolong the king's life, if the occurrence of this amount of building without a builder and organizer is impossible, then the occurrence of the higher and lower worlds with all their wonders and marvels without an existent and manager is even more impossible. So the king awoke from this speech and returned to the true religion.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy (argument from design) for the existence of God. The minister sets up a scenario: he creates a built-up area overnight, then tells the king it appeared by itself. The king rejects that as impossible. The minister then applies the same logic to the universe: if a small palace cannot exist without a builder, the entire universe cannot exist without a Creator and Manager. The king accepts the reasoning and returns to true religion.

Key Terms
وجود الله
حدوث
عمارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the minister actually built the palace himself.
Correction: The minister only ordered the placement of crops, livestock, water, gardens, and palaces to create an illusion; he did not genuinely build them long-term. His purpose was to teach the king a lesson about causality.

Checkpoint
What is the analogy used by the minister to prove God's existence?
A) A palace cannot be built without a builder
B) A garden cannot grow without water
C) A king cannot rule without subjects
D) A feast cannot be prepared without a cook

Key Takeaway
The story illustrates that rational analogy can lead even a doubting king to the truth of God's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were the king in the story, would the minister's analogy truly remove your doubt? What would it take for you to 'awake' like him?
?
Think of a time you doubted God's existence or wisdom. What 'palace' in your life has He built that you cannot deny? Will you acknowledge it today?
?
The minister risked the king's wrath to speak the truth. Whose comfort are you unwilling to disturb for the sake of guiding them to God?

Ja'far al-Sadiq's Question as a Proof
Ja'far al-Sadiq begins a proof by asking about the world's manager.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ja'far al-Sadiq used a leading question to begin a proof for God's existence.

Core Idea
A proof for God begins with a question about the world's manager.

This short passage shows the beginning of a beautiful method of teaching. Instead of giving a direct answer, Ja'far al-Sadiq engages the questioner with a thoughtful question. The response is not included in this edition, but the direction is clear: the world's order points to a manager.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the brevity and the structure of the question.
The passage is just one sentence but carries profound meaning.
2
Understand the context
Ja'far al-Sadiq was asked for a proof (dalil) of God. Instead of a long argument, he replied with this question. What is the purpose of his response?
3
Consider the implication
The question assumes that the world has a manager. Think about how you would complete the argument: if the world has a manager, then God exists. Why might this be effective?
This is a common kalam argument from design.
4
Note the missing answer
The rest of the proof is not in this edition. Reflect on what Ja'far al-Sadiq might have said next. How would you answer his question?
This teaches that proofs can begin with dialogue.

The eighteenth: Ja'far ibn Muhammad al-Sadiq was asked about the proof. He said to the questioner: Tell me about the state of this world: if it had a manager, a director, and a preserver.
