The King's Doubt and the Minister's Analogy
Story of a king's doubt about God resolved by analogy of a built garden.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a simple analogy from creation can serve as a powerful proof for the existence of God.

Core Idea
If a garden needs a builder, the universe needs a Creator.

This story from the Islamic tradition presents a minister using a clever analogy to remove his king's doubt about God's existence. By staging a seemingly miraculous transformation of a barren wilderness, the minister forces the king to confront the logical necessity of a Creator. The passage illustrates a classic cosmological argument from design.

Guided Path
1
Read the story carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the narrative flow before interpreting.
Notice the custom of the annual feast and the minister's plan.
2
Identify the key elements of the analogy
The minister builds a garden in a wilderness and then claims it appeared spontaneously. The king's angry reaction proves the absurdity of such a claim.
3
Understand the logical structure
The minister draws a parallel: if a small garden cannot exist without a builder, the entire universe cannot exist without a Creator. This is a proof by analogy and a form of the argument from design.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The king accepts the logic and returns to true religion. Reflect on why this simple analogy is so effective in removing doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع عشر : كان بعض الملوك قد وقع في قلبه شك في وجود الله تعالى ، وكان له وزير عاقل ، وكانت العادة جارية بأن ذلك الوزير كان يتخذ لذلك الملك ضيافة في كل سنة مرة واحدة فأمر الوزير في بعض المفاوز الخالية بوضع الزرع والضرع فيها وباجراء المياه الجارية وبناء البساتين الطيبة ، ووضع القصور العالية . ثم حضر السلطان في تلك المواضع فتعجب السلطان ، وقال للوزير : كيف عمرت هذه المواضع ؟ فقال الوزير: أطال الله بقاء السلطان إني ما عمرتها ، ولكنها كانت خراباً قفراً الى هذه الأيام القريبة ، ثم إنا لما عدنا وجدنا هذه الأشياء حدثت من تلقاء نفسها من غير بان ولا مصلح ، فاشتد غضب السلطان ، وقال : كيف يليق بمثلك أن يهزأ بي ؟ فقال : أطال الله بقاء السلطان ، إذا كان حدوث هذا القدر من العمارة بلا معمار ولا مصلح ممتنعاً ، فحدوث العالم الأعلى والأسفل مع كثرة ما فيها من العجائب ، والغرائب بلا ولا مدبر أولى بالامتناع . فانتبه السلطان من هذا الكلام ، وعاد إلى موجد الدين الحق .
The seventeenth: There was a king who had a doubt in his heart about the existence of God the Exalted, and he had a wise minister. It was the custom that the minister would prepare a feast for the king once every year. So the minister ordered in some empty wilderness to place crops and livestock there, and to run flowing waters, and to build pleasant gardens, and to erect high palaces. Then the sultan attended those places and was amazed, and said to the minister: How did you build these places? The minister said: May God prolong the life of the sultan, I did not build them, but they were barren deserted until these recent days, then when we returned we found these things came about by themselves without a builder or establisher. The sultan became very angry and said: How is it fitting for the like of you to mock me? He said: May God prolong the life of the sultan, if the occurrence of this amount of building without a builder or establisher is impossible, then the occurrence of the upper and lower world with the multitude of wonders and marvels in it without a creator and manager is more worthy of impossibility. So the sultan awoke from this speech and returned to the founder of the true religion.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a narrative argument (proof by analogy) for the existence of God. It begins by presenting a scenario: a king doubts God's existence, and his wise minister devises a lesson. The minister prepares a barren wilderness and secretly builds gardens, palaces, etc. When the king visits, he is amazed and assumes the minister built them. The minister denies building them, claiming they appeared spontaneously. The king becomes angry at the absurdity. The minister then draws the analogy: if it is impossible for a small area to be built without a builder, then it is even more impossible for the entire universe—with its countless wonders—to exist without a Creator and Manager. The argument is a clear cosmological argument from contingency and design. The king accepts the logic and returns to the true religion.

Key Terms
وجود الله
الدليل
الخالق
المدبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The minister was lying or deceiving the king.
Correction: The minister did not actually claim the garden appeared spontaneously; he used a hypothetical scenario to illustrate the absurdity of believing the universe came about without a Creator.

Checkpoint
What was the minister's main point in the story?
A) That he built the gardens himself by magic.
B) That if a small structure cannot exist without a builder, the universe cannot exist without a Creator.
C) That the king should not doubt God because he is wise.
D) That the minister was playing a trick on the king.

Key Takeaway
The story demonstrates that the existence of a designed universe necessitates a Designer, a form of the cosmological argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the intricate order of the universe—from galaxies to cells—have you ever truly doubted that it all requires a Creator? What would it take for you to honestly confront that doubt instead of avoiding it?
?
The king 'awoke' from his doubt and returned to the founder of the true religion. Is there any area of your faith where you are spiritually asleep? What specific step will you take today to wake up?
?
The minister made the king experience the absurdity of denying a builder. Have you ever allowed yourself to feel the weight of that absurdity? If the universe had no Creator, what would that mean for your own life, purpose, and accountability?

Ja'far al-Sadiq's Proof from the World's Perfection
Ja'far al-Sadiq asked if a manager would improve the world, implying one exists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ja'far al-Sadiq used the world's perfection as a sign of a wise Creator, and how this forms a concise teleological argument.

Core Idea
The world's existing perfection points to a wise manager.

Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq's proof is one of the most concise teleological arguments in Islamic theology. In this passage, he invites the questioner to reflect on the world's condition and what it implies. The reasoning is simple yet profound: if a manager could only improve upon an already perfect system, then the world's perfection itself demands a manager.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the conversational style: the Imam asks a question and guides the questioner to a logical conclusion.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break down the argument: (1) the question, (2) the conditional statement, (3) the implied answer, and (4) the conclusion. The argument is a form of reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the underlying assumption
Consider the assumption that the world is already in its best possible condition. Why does the Imam take this for granted? How does this relate to the argument from design?
This assumption is not explicitly argued here but is based on observation of order and harmony.
4
Connect to the broader teleological argument
Compare this proof to other teleological arguments you may have encountered, such as those based on order, purpose, or fine-tuning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن عشر : سئل جعفر بن محمد [ الصادق] عن الدليل فقال للسائل : أخبرني عن حال هذا العالم ، لو كان له مدبر [ ومباشر] (٥) وحافظ . أما كان يزيد حاله حينئذ على هذه الأحوال الموجودة ؟ وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فهذه الأحوال وجب أن تكون دالة على أن لها [ إلها] (۱) مديراً حكيماً .
The eighteenth: Ja'far ibn Muhammad [al-Sadiq] was asked about the proof (daleel), so he said to the questioner: Inform me about the condition of this world, if it had a manager [and supervisor] and guardian, would its condition then increase over these existing conditions? And if the matter is so, then these conditions must indicate that they have a god, a wise manager.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents a brief proof (daleel) for the existence of a wise Creator attributed to Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq. The proof is teleological: the world currently exhibits a certain condition (order, perfection). The Imam asks the questioner to consider: if the world had a manager, supervisor, and guardian, would its condition be better than it currently is? The implied answer is yes. Since it is already perfect, it must actually have such a manager. The logic is that the observed perfection of the world is best explained by a wise manager, not by chance. This is a concise version of the argument from design.

Key Terms
الدليل
مدبر
حكيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument fails because the world contains evil and imperfection.
Correction: Ja'far al-Sadiq's proof focuses on the overall order and harmony of the world, not on absolute perfection. The presence of some evil does not negate the general design evident in the cosmos.

Checkpoint
What does Ja'far al-Sadiq's proof rely on?
A) The world is full of evil, so it needs a manager to fix it.
B) The world's current condition is so good that it must be managed by a wise being.
C) The world cannot exist without a guardian.
D) The questioner must admit he does not know.

Key Takeaway
Ja'far al-Sadiq's proof shows that the world's existing perfection is itself a sign of a wise Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the order in your own life—your health, your provision, the sequence of events—do you truly see them as the work of a wise Manager, or do you attribute them to coincidence? What would change if you consistently saw His hand in everything?
?
If someone asked you to point to the clearest evidence that the universe has a Manager, what would you say? Have you ever personally felt the weight of that evidence in a way that moved you to gratitude or trust?
?
The proof states that the world's conditions themselves indicate a wise Manager. Is there any area of your life where you resist that management—where you wish to be independent? How does this proof challenge your desire for autonomy?

The Astrologer and the King's Thought: An Analogy for God's Existence
Story of al-Muwaffaq testing astrologers with a hidden thought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the story of al-Muwaffaq and the astrologers uses an astrological analogy to argue for God's existence from His effects.

Core Idea
The invisible God is known through His visible effects.

This passage recounts a famous story where a king tests astrologers by hiding a thought. Only one astrologer correctly guesses it, not by mind-reading, but by reading the celestial signs. His explanation becomes an analogy: just as an invisible celestial point reveals itself through its effects, so does the invisible God reveal Himself through creation. This story is a powerful illustration of the argument from effects (burhan al-athar) for God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the story carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the narrative. Notice the challenge, the failure of the astrologers, and the success of Abu Ma'shar.
The story is a narrative, so pay attention to the sequence of events.
2
Identify key elements
Identify the king's challenge, the method used by the astrologers (extracting the ascendant), and Abu Ma'shar's specific reasoning.
Note that Abu Ma'shar did not use ordinary astrology but a specific point called 'point of the head'.
3
Understand the analogy
Focus on Abu Ma'shar's explanation: the vertex is invisible but its effects are seen; similarly, God's essence is invisible but His effects (goodness, mercy) are seen.
The analogy connects a celestial phenomenon to divine attributes.
4
Recognize the theological purpose
Understand that the story is not endorsing astrology but using it as an analogy for the rational inference from creation to Creator.
The argument is similar to the cosmological argument from contingency.
5
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how this analogy strengthens the argument for God's existence from observable effects in the world.

العشرون : روي أن الموفق بالله لما حجّ ، وكان قد حضر عنده جمع من المنجمين مثل أبي معشر البلخي ، وما شاء (٥) الله وغيرهما . فقال لهم : إني اسمع أنكم تدعون أن الإنسان يضمر في قلبه ضميراً ، وأنتم تستخرجونه. وأنا أضمرت الآن ضميراً فاستخرجوه . ثم إن أولئك المنجمين استخرجوا طالع الوقت ، وقال كل واحد منهم كلاما ، فلم يوافق كلام أحد فقال أبو معشر البلخي : أضمرت ذكرت الله . فقال : صدقت . فأخبرني كيف عرفت ؟ فقال : لأنك لما أضمرت أخذت ارتفاع الوقت فوجدت نقطة الرأس وسط السماء ، ونقطة الرأس شيء لا يرى ذاته ، ويرى آثار خيره ورحمته [ ووسط السماء أرفع موضع في الفلك . فعرفت أنك أضمرت ذکر موجود لا ترى ذاته ویری آثار خيره ورحمته ] (۸) وذلك الموجود هو أرفعهم
The twentieth: It is narrated that al-Muwaffaq Billah, when he performed Hajj, and there was present with him a group of astrologers such as Abu Ma'shar al-Balkhi and Masha'allah and others. He said to them: I hear that you claim that a person conceals something in his heart, and you extract it. Now I have concealed a thought in my heart, so extract it. Then those astrologers extracted the ascendant of the time, and each of them said something, but none of their words matched. Then Abu Ma'shar al-Balkhi said: You concealed 'I remembered Allah'. He said: You have told the truth. So tell me how you knew. He said: Because when you concealed, I took the altitude of the time and found the point of the head (vertex) in the middle of the sky, and the point of the head is something whose essence cannot be seen, but the effects of its goodness and mercy are seen, and the middle of the sky is the highest place in the sphere. So I knew that you concealed the remembrance of an Existent whose essence is not seen, but the effects of His goodness and mercy are seen. And that Existent is the highest of them.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a narrative illustrating a proof for God's existence through astrological analogy. Al-Muwaffaq Billah tests astrologers by thinking of something in his heart. The astrologers fail except Abu Ma'shar al-Balkhi, who correctly identifies that the king thought 'I remembered Allah'. Abu Ma'shar explains his method: he took the astrological ascendant and observed that the 'point of the head' (a point in the sky) was at the zenith. This point is invisible in essence but its effects (goodness and mercy) are visible. Similarly, the king's thought was about an Existent (God) whose essence is invisible but whose effects are visible. The story uses astrological symbolism to draw an analogy: the position of the vertex in the sky points to an invisible entity whose effects are manifest; likewise, the universe points to an invisible God whose effects (creation, mercy) are manifest. The argument is that knowledge of God can be inferred from His effects, similar to how astrologers infer hidden things from celestial signs.

الآثار
ذكر الله

Misconception: This story proves that astrology is a valid method for knowing the unseen.
Correction: The story uses astrology only as an analogy; it does not validate astrological practices. The real argument is about inferring the unseen Creator from visible effects.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The story of al-Muwaffaq elegantly demonstrates that just as the invisible celestial vertex is known by its effects, so the existence of the invisible God is known through His creation.

The author concludes that the highest existent is only Allah and notes the abundance of similar arguments.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
The highest existent is none other than Allah.

This passage is the conclusion of a prior argument that established the existence of a highest existent. The author now states plainly that this highest existent is Allah, and notes that the reader has already implicitly acknowledged this. He also remarks that many similar arguments exist, and the one provided serves as a pointer to the rest.

The Historians' Proof from Universal Consensus
The author presents the first group of scholars and their argument from universal consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the historians' argument from universal consensus—that all nations agree on the existence of a manager of the world—serves as a rational proof for God's existence.

Core Idea
All people, even those who reject prophethood, agree that a Supreme Being manages the world.

The author begins by categorizing scholars and their methods. The first group he presents are historians who study the records of past nations. Their first argument is striking: despite disagreements on attributes, every significant group across history has acknowledged a being that governs the universe. Let's examine how this consensus is documented across different traditions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classification
The author first states that sciences are many and each scholar has a method. Note that he is enumerating these groups.
This sets the stage for the first group.
2
Identify the first group and their proof
The first group are historians. Their proof has two aspects; the first aspect is the argument from universal consensus.
Focus on the first aspect for now.
3
Examine the evidence from each religion
The author provides examples from Muslims, Jews, Christians, Magians, and Persians. Read each example carefully.
Notice how each group affirms a supreme being despite differences.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The consensus shows that belief in a manager of the world is universal. This is a rational proof because it is based on observable historical fact.
Think about why this is a powerful argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن أصناف العلوم وأقسامها كثيرة، ولكل واحد من أصناف العلماء طريق يختص به في إثبات معرفة الله تعالى. ونحن نعدها: الطائفة الأولى: العلماء الباحثون عن تواريخ أهل الدنيا، ومعرفة الأحوال الماضية من أحوال هذا العالم. ودليل هذا الصنف من العلماء على إثبات الإله لهذا العالم من وجهين. الأول: قالوا إن البحث عن تواريخ أهل الدنيا يدل على أنه لم يوجد تحت قبة السماء طائفة منهم كثيرة معتبرة ينكرون وجود الله تعالى. وإنما النزاع الواقع بين الخلق في الصفات. أما الاعتراف بوجود شيء يدبر هذا العالم فأمر متفق عليه بين الكل. والذي يدل عليه: أن أهل العالم فريقان: منهم من يعترف بنبوة الأنبياء ومنهم من ينكر النبوة. أما المعترفون بنبوة الأنبياء عليهم السلام فلا نزاع في أنهم معترفون بوجود الله تعالى. أما المسلمون فالأمر بينهم ظاهر، وأما اليهود فاعترافهم بوجود الإله تعالى أظهر من أن يحتاج إلى البيان والذكر. والذي يذكرونه كثيراً قولهم: "ألوهيم أدوناي آهيا شراهيا". وكل ما في التوراة من معجزات موسى عليه السلام فهو بعينه دليل وجود الإله تعالى، وكان موسى يقول: الحكم هو الذي أتى بهذه العجائب، وأظهر عجائب الغرائب. وأما النصارى فاعترافهم بوجود الإله ظاهر، ويقولون: "أبداً، إلها، ربا، قديساً"، والتفاوت بين السريانية وبين العربية قليل. وأما المجوس: فإنهم يسمون الله تعالى بلغتهم: "أهر من، أو يزدان". يزعمون: أنهم إنما جعلوا النار قبلة صلاتهم، لأن النار جسم مشرف عالي قاهر، وهذه الصفات تتناسب مناسبة غير بعيدة لصفات جلال الله من بعض الوجوه، فلهذا السبب جعلوا النار قبلة صلاتهم، وخصوها بمزيد من التعظيم. وقال زردشت في كتابه زند اوستا، وهو الكتاب الذي زعم أن الله أنزله عليه: "وتبدو لهم بهيئة أهر من". والأعاجم كانوا يقولون: خداي: وترجمته بالعربية: أنه بنفسه جاء يعني أنه بذاته وجد، وحصل. وتفسيره: أنه واجب الوجود لذاته.
Know that the categories of sciences and their divisions are many, and for each one of the categories of scholars there is a method specific to it in establishing knowledge of God the Most High. And we enumerate them: the first group: the scholars who research the histories of the people of the world, and knowledge of past conditions from the circumstances of this world. And the proof of this category of scholars for establishing the deity for this world is from two aspects. The first: they said that research into the histories of the people of the world indicates that there has not existed under the dome of the sky a group of them—many and considerable—who deny the existence of God the Most High. Rather, the dispute that occurs among creation is concerning the attributes. As for the acknowledgment of the existence of something that manages this world, it is a matter agreed upon by all. And what indicates it is that the people of the world are two groups: among them are those who acknowledge the prophethood of the prophets, and among them are those who deny prophethood. As for those who acknowledge the prophethood of the prophets, peace be upon them, there is no dispute that they acknowledge the existence of God the Most High. As for the Muslims, the matter is apparent among them; and as for the Jews, their acknowledgment of the existence of the deity the Most High is more evident than to need explanation and mention. And that which they mention frequently is their saying: "Elohim, Adonai, Ehyeh Asher Ehyeh." And everything in the Torah of the miracles of Moses, peace be upon him, is precisely proof of the existence of the deity the Most High, and Moses used to say: "The Judge is the one who brought these wonders and manifested wondrous marvels." And as for the Christians, their acknowledgment of the existence of the deity is apparent, and they say: "Everlasting, God, Lord, Holy," and the discrepancy between Syriac and Arabic is slight. And as for the Magians: they name God the Most High in their language: "Ahriman, or Yazdan." They claim that they only made fire the direction of their prayer because fire is a noble, high, overwhelming body, and these attributes correspond by no distant correspondence to the attributes of the majesty of God in some respects, so for this reason they made fire the direction of their prayer and singled it out for increased veneration. And Zoroaster said in his book Zend Avesta—which is the book that he claimed God revealed to him: "And it appears to them in the form of Ahriman." And the Persians used to say: "Khudai"—and its translation in Arabic: that He came by Himself, meaning that He exists by His essence and occurred; and its interpretation: that He is necessary of existence by His essence.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a classification and a proof. The author begins by stating that the categories of sciences are numerous, and each category of scholars has its own method for proving the knowledge of God. He then starts enumerating them, beginning with the first group: the historians who study the histories and past conditions of the world's inhabitants. Their proof for establishing a deity for this world has two aspects. The author here presents the first aspect, which is the argument from universal consensus. The historians argue that investigating the histories of the world's people shows that no significant and sizable group has ever denied the existence of God. Disputes among people are only about the attributes of God, not about the existence of a being who manages the universe, which is agreed upon by everyone. To support this, he divides the people of the world into two groups: those who accept prophethood and those who reject it. Those who accept prophethood—like Muslims, Jews, and Christians—clearly affirm God's existence. He gives specific examples: the Jews use names like 'Elohim, Adonai, Ehyeh Asher Ehyeh' and regard the miracles of Moses as proof; Christians acknowledge God with terms like 'Everlasting, God, Lord, Holy'; the Magians (Zoroastrians) call God 'Ahriman' or 'Yazdan' and venerate fire as a symbol due to its noble qualities; and the Persians use the term 'Khudai', which means 'necessary of existence by His essence'. Thus, the author demonstrates that all groups, even those who reject prophethood, still acknowledge a supreme being. This consensus across all nations and religions serves as the first proof for the existence of God.

Four Monotheistic Groups Affirm God's Existence
A survey of four monotheistic groups and their consensus on God's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how four major monotheistic groups—Muslims, Jews, Christians, and Magians—each affirm the existence of God, and that their consensus demonstrates a universal recognition of the divine.

Core Idea
All four monotheistic traditions agree: God exists.

This lesson surveys the testimony of four influential religious communities that acknowledge prophethood. Each group—Muslims, Jews, Christians, and Magians—has its own way of naming and describing God, yet they all converge on the fundamental truth that God exists. The author presents their diverse expressions to show that belief in God is not limited to one tradition but is a shared human conviction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from the matn
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow. Notice how the author moves from one group to the next, ending with a statement of consensus.
The passage lists four groups in a specific order.
2
Identify the four groups and their testimonies
Pause after each group and note what the author says about their affirmation of God. For each, identify the divine names or terms they use.
Jews: Elohim, Adonai, Ehyeh. Christians: God, Lord, Holy. Magians: Ahriman, Yazdan. Muslims: implicit.
3
Analyze the Magian case carefully
The Magians are the most distinctive group, using fire as a symbol. Read their section again and understand why they use fire and what they call God.
The author clarifies that fire is a qibla, not an object of worship.
4
Connect to the overall argument
After reading the entire passage, ask yourself: what does this survey accomplish? How does it support the broader discussion of God's existence?
The author builds a cumulative case: even different traditions agree.
5
Reflect on the consensus
Notice the concluding sentence: all four groups are 'unanimously agreed upon affirming the existence of God Almighty.' This is the key takeaway.

بوجود الله تعالى . أما المسلمون فالأمر بينهم ظاهر ، وأما اليهود فاعترافهم بوجود الإله تعالى أظهر من أن يحتاج الى البيان والذكر . والذي يذكرونه كثيراً قولهم : ألوهيم . أدوناي . آهيا شراهياً. وكل ما في التوراة من معجزات موسى عليه السلام فهو بعينه دليل وجود الإله تعالى، وكان موسى يقول : الحكم هو الذي أتى بهذه العجائب ، وأظهر عجائب الغرائب ، وأما النصارى فاعترافهم بوجود الإله ظاهر ، ويقولون : أبداً، إلهاً، ربا، قديساً، والتفاوت بين السريانية وبين العربية قليل . وأما المجوس : فإنهم يسمون الله تعالى بلغتهم : أهر من ، أو يزدان . يزعمون : أنهم إنما جعلوا النار قبلة صلاتهم. لأن النار جسم مشرف عالي قاهر ، وهذه الصفات تتناسب مناسبة غير بعيدة لصفات جلال الله من بعض الوجوه ، فلهذا السبب جعلوا النار قبلة صلاتهم، وخصوها بمزيد من التعظيم ، وقال زردشت في كتابه زند اوستا ، وهو الكتاب الذي زعم أن الله أنزله عليه : وتبدو لهم بهيئة أهر من . والأعاجم كانوا يقولون : خداي : وتفسيره : أنه واجب الوجود لذاته . فهذه الطوائف الأربع وهم الطوائف المعتبرون ممن يقر بنبوة الأنبياء عليهم السلام كلهم مطبقون متفقون على الإقرار بوجود الله تعالى
By the existence of God Almighty. As for the Muslims, the matter is obvious among them. As for the Jews, their acknowledgment of the existence of God Almighty is too clear to need explanation and mention. What they often mention is their saying: Elohim, Adonai, Ehyeh. And everything in the Torah of the miracles of Moses, peace be upon him, is itself a proof of the existence of God Almighty, and Moses used to say: The Judge is the one who brought these wonders and manifested wondrous marvels. As for the Christians, their acknowledgment of the existence of God is apparent, and they say: Everlasting, God, Lord, Holy. The difference between Syriac and Arabic is slight. As for the Magians, they call God Almighty in their language: Ahriman or Yazdan. They claim that they made fire the qibla of their prayer because fire is an honorable, lofty, overpowering body, and these attributes have a not-distant correspondence to the attributes of God's majesty in some respects; for this reason they made fire the qibla of their prayer and singled it out with great veneration. Zoroaster said in his book Zand Avesta, the book he claimed God revealed to him: 'And He appears to them in the form of Ahriman.' The non-Arabs used to say: Khuda, and its interpretation is that He is necessary of existence by Himself. So these four groups — and they are the respected groups among those who affirm the prophethood of the prophets, peace be upon them — are all unanimously agreed upon affirming the existence of God Almighty.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a survey of the four major monotheistic groups that acknowledge prophethood: Muslims, Jews, Christians, and Magians. The author begins by stating that for Muslims the affirmation of God's existence is obvious. For Jews, their acknowledgment is even more evident, and they mention the divine names Elohim, Adonai, and Ehyeh; moreover, the miracles of Moses in the Torah are themselves proofs of God's existence, and Moses attributed those wonders to the Judge (God). For Christians, their confession is clear, as they address God as everlasting, God, Lord, Holy; the author notes the close linguistic relationship between Syriac and Arabic. For the Magians (Zoroastrians), they call God Ahriman or Yazdan; they claim they made fire their prayer direction because fire is a lofty, powerful body that remotely resembles attributes of God's majesty. Zoroaster claimed that God appears to them in the form of Ahriman. The author then mentions that the non-Arabs (Persians) used the term 'Khuda' to mean the self-necessary being. The passage concludes with a summary: these four groups, which are the respected communities that affirm prophethood, are unanimously agreed upon affirming the existence of God Almighty. The author follows the order of each group, presenting their specific testimony to God's existence, and then synthesizes their consensus. The argument is cumulative: each group's acknowledgment reinforces the universal recognition of God.

D) Magians

Key Takeaway
Four monotheistic groups—Muslims, Jews, Christians, and Magians—despite their differences, unanimously affirm the existence of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even non-Muslims affirm God's existence. If God is so clearly evident, why do you still struggle at times to feel His presence in your daily life? What concrete step can you take today to remove a distraction that veils your awareness of Him?
?
The Magians direct their prayers toward fire because of its powerful and lofty attributes. Do you ever find yourself directing your devotion toward something other than God—like wealth, status, or relationships—because it feels powerful or immediate? How can you realign your heart's qibla exclusively toward Him?
?
Jewish, Christian, and Zoroastrian scriptures and practices all point to the same God, yet you have been blessed with the clearest guidance through the Quran and Sunnah. How does this privilege weigh on your soul? What is one way you can live today that shows you truly value this gift over the lesser paths others follow?

Universal Belief in God: Five Nations' Testimony
Author lists five non-monotheistic groups affirming God's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the testimony of five non-monotheistic groups to strengthen the argument for God's existence through universal consensus.

Core Idea
Belief in a supreme being is universal, even among those farthest from revelation.

The author continues his argument from universal consensus by citing groups outside the Abrahamic traditions. These groups—pre-Islamic Arabs, Indians, Zanj, Turks, and Chinese—all, despite their polytheism or perceived primitiveness, affirm the existence of a supreme creator. This passage shows that belief in God is not limited to monotheistic religions but is ingrained in human nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the author’s method of enumerating each group with their specific testimony.
The passage uses the word 'مطبقون' (unanimously affirming) for each group.
2
Identify each group and their testimony
Go through each of the five groups: pre-Islamic Arabs, Indians, Zanj, Turks, and Chinese. For each, note what they call God and how they express their belief, even if through idolatry or extreme acts.
The Zanj and Turks use non-Arabic terms (Malkari Jalwi, Beh Yinkari) showing indigenous recognition of God.
3
Reflect on the universal consensus argument
Ask yourself: Why does the author include groups that are not monotheistic? How does their testimony support the claim that belief in God is innate and universal?
This lesson shows that even groups considered 'farthest from reason' (Zanj) still acknowledge a Supreme Lord.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما سائر الطوائف : فأحدها : أهل الجاهلية : وهم العرب الذين كانوا موجودين قبل ظهور الإسلام، وكلهم كانوا مطبقين على الإقرار بوجود الإله والدليل عليه قوله تعالى حكاية عنهم : ( ولئن سألتهم من خلق السموات والأرض ؟ ليقولن : الله ) وقال تعالى : ( أفي الله شك ؟ فاطر السموات والأرض) وكانوا يطلقون لفظ الإله على الأوثان ، أما لفظ الله فما كانوا يذكرونه إلا في حق الله تعالى . والصِنف الثاني : الهند، وكلهم مطبقون على الإقرار بوجود الإله الحكيم الرحيم، ولذلك فإنهم يبالغون في العبادة ويحرقون أنفسهم بالنار، على حب الله تعالى . والصِنف الثالث : الزنوج وهم أبعد طوائف أهل الدنيا عن العقل. ومع ذلك فهم مقرون بوجود إله العالم، ويقولون : ملکری جلوی ومعناه : الرب الأعظم . والصِنف الرابع : الترك، وهم مقرون بوجود الإله تعالى فيقولون : به ينكري، يعني الرب واحد، وقد يقول بعضهم : إلخ بايات. ومعناه الغني الأعظم . والصِنف الخامس : أهل الصين. ولهم على عظيم من الإقرار بوجود الإله المدير الحكيم
As for the remaining groups: The first of them: the people of the Jahiliyyah — they are the Arabs who existed before the advent of Islam, and all of them were unanimously affirming the existence of the deity. The proof for it is His statement, the Exalted, narrating from them: 'And if you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will surely say: Allah.' And the Exalted said: 'Is there doubt about Allah, the Originator of the heavens and the earth?' And they used to apply the word 'ilah' (deity) to idols, but as for the word 'Allah', they would not mention it except for God Almighty. The second group: the Indians, and all of them are unanimously affirming the existence of the Wise, Compassionate God; therefore they exaggerate in worship and burn themselves with fire out of love for God Almighty. The third group: the Zanj (black Africans), and they are the farthest groups of the people of the world from reason. Yet despite that, they affirm the existence of the God of the world, and they say: 'Malkari Jalwi,' meaning: the Supreme Lord. The fourth group: the Turks, and they affirm the existence of God Almighty and say: 'Beh Yinkari,' meaning: the Lord is One, and some of them may say: 'Ilkh Bayat,' meaning: the Supreme Rich. The fifth group: the Chinese, and they have a great affirmation of the existence of the wise, managing God.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the argument from universal consensus, listing five additional groups that affirm God's existence. The author proceeds in order: first, the pre-Islamic Arabs (Jahiliyyah) who, despite their idolatry, acknowledged Allah as the creator, as evidenced by Quranic verses. They used 'ilah' for idols but reserved 'Allah' for the true God. Second, the Hindus, who affirm a wise and merciful God and even burn themselves out of love. Third, the Zanj (sub-Saharan Africans), considered the farthest from reason, yet they still acknowledge a supreme Lord, calling Him 'Malkari Jalwi'. Fourth, the Turks, who affirm God as one and call Him 'Beh Yinkari' or 'Ilkh Bayat'. Fifth, the Chinese, who have a great affirmation of a wise, managing God. The author's structure is enumerative, listing each group with their specific testimony. The key point is that even groups far from revealed religion or considered primitive still possess a fundamental belief in a supreme being, which reinforces the argument that belief in God is innate and universal. The passage ends with the Chinese group, leaving the cumulative proof clear: from the most civilized to the most remote, all peoples acknowledge a creator and lord.

أهل الصين

ملکری جلوی

إلخ بايات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only monotheistic religions (Judaism, Christianity, Islam) believe in God; other groups are purely atheistic or polytheistic without any concept of a supreme being.
Correction: This passage shows that even polytheistic and primitive groups (pre-Islamic Arabs, Indians, Zanj, Turks, Chinese) all affirm the existence of a supreme creator or lord, though they may associate partners with Him.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group is described as 'the farthest groups of the people of the world from reason' yet still affirms God's existence?
A) Pre-Islamic Arabs
B) Indians
C) Zanj
D) Chinese

Key Takeaway
The testimony of five diverse groups—Arabs, Indians, Zanj, Turks, and Chinese—demonstrates that belief in a supreme being is a universal human phenomenon, strengthening the argument for God's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Zanj are described as 'the farthest groups from reason,' yet they still affirm a Supreme Lord. You may not be 'far from reason,' but do you live as if God is truly supreme over every aspect of your life? What would change if you actually believed that with the same certainty as those who have no revelation?
?
The pre-Islamic Arabs acknowledged Allah as the Creator but worshipped idols alongside Him. Is there any 'idol' in your life—money, status, comfort, relationships—that you acknowledge God as Creator but still give ultimate authority to? What step can you take today to tear down that idol?
?
The Indians would burn themselves with fire out of love for God, even though their concept of God is incomplete. How deep is your love for the true God? When was the last time you sacrificed something significant purely out of love for Him? Are you willing to ask Him to increase that love, even if it costs you?

The Universal Consensus on God's Existence
Enumeration of groups affirming God's existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses historical and cultural evidence to show that belief in God is universal across all known civilizations.

Core Idea
Every culture in history has affirmed the existence of a supreme deity.

The author begins by listing various nations and tribes from around the world, demonstrating that belief in a supreme being is not limited to one region or religion. From pre-Islamic Arabs to Greeks, all have acknowledged a creator. This lesson surveys the evidence he presents for each group.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of groups
Read the Arabic passage that lists the seven categories: pre-Islamic Arabs, Indians, Zanj, Turks, Chinese, Romans/Berbers/Copts/Abyssinians, and Greeks.
Notice that each group is described with specific evidence of their belief.
2
Identify the evidence for each group
For each group, note the author's proof: for Arabs he quotes Quran, for Indians/others he references their practices and names for God.
3
Reflect on the universality of belief
Consider that the author claims all these diverse groups, across time and geography, agreed on God's existence. How does that strengthen the argument?
This is the foundation for the next lesson's logical conclusion.

بوجود الإله تعالى والصنف السابع : اليونانيون، وهم كانوا كالمشعوذين بهذا العلم [ والعمل عليه ] وما طلبوا علماً من العلوم الدقيقة ولا بغوا بحثاً من المباحث النفيسة إلا ليتوسلوا به إلى معرفة [ وجود الإله تعالى ومعرفة ] صفات كبريائه الإله فهذا هو ضبط [ أصناف ] أهل الدنيا ، وكلهم مطبقون على وجود الإله والتواريخ القديمة دالة على أن أهل الدنيا من الدهر الداهر ، والزمان الأقدم ، هكذا كانوا وما كان فيهم أحد ينكر وجود الإله تعالى . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن من المعلوم بالضرورة أن عقل [ جميع] أهل المشرق والمغرب في مدة سبعة آلاف سنة ، أو أقل ، أو أكثر : أزيد من عقل واحد مغمور بين الخلق . فعلى هذا لو اتفق لإنسان واحد شبهة ، أوشك في وجود الإله [ تعالى] فيجب أن يقطع بأن ذلك الشك أو الشبهة لقصور عقله وقلة فهمه ، لا لعدم المطلوب. فإن صريح العقل شاهد بأن عقول جملة الخلق في هذه الدهور المتطاولة ، كانت أكمل من عقل هذا الواحد . فهذا طريق قوي جلي في إثبات العلم بوجود الإله الحكيم ، بشرط أن يعترف الإنسان بان عقله أقل من عقل الكل [ونسأل الله الرحمة ]
These four groups — and they are the groups considered among those who affirm the prophethood of the prophets, peace be upon them — all are in agreement and unanimous upon affirming the existence of God Almighty. As for the remaining groups: The first: the people of pre-Islamic ignorance: they are the Arabs who existed before the advent of Islam, and all of them were united upon affirming the existence of the deity. The evidence for that is His statement, relating from them: 'And if you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will surely say: Allah.' And He said: 'Is there doubt about Allah, the Originator of the heavens and the earth?' They used to apply the word 'ilah' to idols, but the word 'Allah' they would only mention with regard to God Almighty. The second category of the people of the world: India, and all of them are united upon affirming the existence of the wise, merciful deity, and therefore they go to extremes in worship and burn themselves with fire out of love for God Almighty. The third category of the people of the world: the Zanj (Black Africans), and they are the farthest groups of the people of the world from reason. Yet they affirm the existence of the Lord of the world, and they say: 'Malikri Jalu' meaning: the Supreme Lord. The fourth category of the people of the world: the Turks, and they affirm the existence of God Almighty, so they say: 'Beh Yankri', meaning the Lord is one, and some of them say: 'Ilkh Bayat' meaning the Supreme Self-Sufficient. The fifth category: the Chinese. They have great affirmation of the existence of the wise director deity. The sixth category: the Romans, Berbers, Copts, and Abyssinians, and the majority of these are Christians; among them are Muslims. In any case, they affirm the existence of God Almighty. The seventh category: the Greeks, and they were like sorcerers with this knowledge and practice upon it. They did not seek any of the precise sciences nor pursue any valuable investigation except to use it as a means to know the existence of God Almighty and to know the attributes of His divine majesty. This is the classification of the categories of the people of the world, and all of them are united upon the existence of the deity. Ancient histories indicate that the people of the world from time immemorial and the most ancient age were thus, and there was none among them who denied the existence of God Almighty. And when this is established, we say: It is known by necessity that the intellect of all the people of the East and the West over a period of seven thousand years, or less, or more, is greater than the intellect of a single individual hidden among creation. Therefore, if a single person experiences a doubt or uncertainty about the existence of God Almighty, it must be concluded that that doubt or uncertainty is due to the deficiency of his intellect and the poverty of his understanding, not due to the absence of the object of seeking. For clear reason testifies that the intellects of all creation in these long eras were more perfect than the intellect of this one. This is a strong and clear way to establish knowledge of the existence of the wise deity, on the condition that a person acknowledges that his intellect is less than the intellect of all [and we ask God for mercy].
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of God based on universal consensus (ijmāʿ). The author first enumerates various nations and groups throughout history, all of whom unanimously affirmed the existence of God. He begins by mentioning the four categories of people who accept prophethood, then lists other categories: pre-Islamic Arabs, Indians, Zanj (Black Africans), Turks, Chinese, Romans/Berbers/Copts/Abyssinians, and Greeks. For each, he provides evidence of their belief in a supreme deity, often citing their own terminology. The author then argues that since all people from the earliest times up to seven thousand years (or more) have agreed on this truth, their collective intellect is far superior to that of any single individual who might doubt. Therefore, if one person has a doubt, it must be due to his own intellectual deficiency, not because the truth is lacking. This is presented as a strong and clear proof (ṭarīq qawī jallī) for affirming the existence of God, provided one humbly admits that his intellect is less than that of the entire human race. The final bracketed phrase 'we ask God for mercy' is a scribal addition.

وجود الله
إله
طوائف
شبهة

Collective Intellect and the Refutation of Doubt
Argument from collective intellect against individual doubt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that universal consensus provides a stronger basis for certainty than individual doubt.

Core Idea
The collective intellect of all humanity outweighs a single person's doubt.

Having established that all human groups throughout history have affirmed God, the author now presents a powerful logical argument: if one person doubts, it must be due to his own mental deficiency, not because the truth is absent. This lesson examines that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument from universal consensus
Read the passage starting from 'وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول' which presents the conclusion that collective intellect over 7,000 years is greater than any individual intellect.
2
Understand the premise
The author assumes that the intellect of all people in the East and West over thousands of years is cumulative and superior to that of a single person.
This is an a priori assumption, not empirically proven.
3
Analyze the conclusion
If one person experiences doubt, it must be due to his own deficiency (قصور عقله) not because the object of seeking (God) is absent.
4
Reflect on the condition
The author adds a condition: one must admit that his intellect is less than the intellect of all. This is a call to humility.
This condition makes the argument self-referential.

The seventh category: the Greeks, and they were like sorcerers with this knowledge and practice upon it. They did not seek any of the precise sciences nor pursue any valuable investigation except to use it as a means to know the existence of God Almighty and to know the attributes of His divine majesty. This is the classification of the categories of the people of the world, and all of them are united upon the existence of the deity. Ancient histories indicate that the people of the world from time immemorial and the most ancient age were thus, and there was none among them who denied the existence of God Almighty. And when this is established, we say: It is known by necessity that the intellect of all the people of the East and the West over a period of seven thousand years, or less, or more, is greater than the intellect of a single individual hidden among creation. Therefore, if a single person experiences a doubt or uncertainty about the existence of God Almighty, it must be concluded that that doubt or uncertainty is due to the deficiency of his intellect and the poverty of his understanding, not due to the absence of the object of seeking. For clear reason testifies that the intellects of all creation in these long eras were more perfect than the intellect of this one. This is a strong and clear way to establish knowledge of the existence of the wise deity, on the condition that a person acknowledges that his intellect is less than the intellect of all [and we ask God for mercy].
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of God based on universal consensus (ijmāʿ). The author first enumerates various nations and groups throughout history, all of whom unanimously affirmed the existence of God. He begins by mentioning the four categories of people who accept prophethood, then lists other categories: pre-Islamic Arabs, Indians, Zanj (Black Africans), Turks, Chinese, Romans/Berbers/Copts/Abyssinians, and Greeks. For each, he provides evidence of their belief in a supreme deity, often citing their own terminology. The author then argues that since all people from the earliest times up to seven thousand years (or more) have agreed on this truth, their collective intellect is far superior to that of any single individual who might doubt. Therefore, if one person has a doubt, it must be due to his own intellectual deficiency, not because the truth is lacking. This is presented as a strong and clear proof (ṭarīq qawī jallī) for affirming the existence of God, provided one humbly admits that his intellect is less than that of the entire human race. The final bracketed phrase 'we ask God for mercy' is a scribal addition.

Key Terms
عقل
شبهة
طريق قوي جلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is based on majority opinion, not unanimity.
Correction: The author explicitly states that all groups (جميع أهل الدنيا) from ancient times agreed, making it a universal consensus, not a mere majority.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what must a person acknowledge in order for this proof to be valid?
A) That God exists
B) That his intellect is less than the intellect of all
C) That doubt is permissible
D) That the universe is eternal

Key Takeaway
When all of humanity agrees on a truth, a single dissenter's doubt reflects his own limitation, not the truth's absence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself doubting a truth that billions of people across history have held, does it ever occur to you that the problem might be your own intellect rather than the truth? How often do you sincerely humble yourself before the collective wisdom of humanity?
?
You claim to believe in God, but in your daily life, do you act as though His existence is more certain than your own fleeting thoughts? Or do you secretly trust your own reasoning more than the unanimous conviction of all the prophets and believers?
?
If you truly admitted that your intellect is less than the intellect of all, what specific doubts or objections about your faith would you need to reconsider? Are you willing to abandon those doubts as symptoms of your own deficiency, or will you cling to them out of pride?

Allah's Existence
beginner
5 min
The Universal Consensus Argument for God's Existence
The author lists two categories of nations affirming God and argues from universal consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument for God's existence based on universal consensus across history and cultures, and how it uses the premise of collective intellectual superiority.

Core Idea
Collective human intellect over millennia is greater than any single doubter's mind.

This passage presents a compelling proof for God's existence that relies not on abstract philosophy but on the historical fact that all nations, across ages, have affirmed a supreme being. After enumerating the major civilizations and their belief, the author draws a logical conclusion: if one person doubts, his intellect must be deficient. Let's explore this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice the author's enumeration style and the logical turn at 'wa idha thabata hadha...'.
The passage uses historical examples to build a universal claim.
2
Identify the two categories of nations
Find the sixth and seventh categories. The sixth includes Romans, Berbers, Copts, Abyssinians; the seventh is Greeks. Note that despite differences, they all affirm God.
The author lists these to show diversity of belief systems united on this point.
3
Understand the consensus claim
The author asserts that all people of the world, from ancient times, have agreed on God's existence. Historical records confirm no denial. This sets up the premise.
Consensus is not just majority; it is universal across time and space.
4
Grasp the logical inference
The collective intellect of all people over seven thousand years is greater than any one person's. Therefore, if one person doubts, it must be due to his deficiency, not lack of evidence.
This is a probabilistic argument based on the reliability of many minds.
5
Reflect on the condition
The argument works only if the doubter admits his intellect is less than the collective. This humility is necessary for the proof to be convincing.
The condition acknowledges human fallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصنف السادس : الروم والبربر ، والقبط ، والحبشة ، وهؤلاء الغالب عليهم النصرانية . وفيهم المسلمون . وعلى التقدير فهم مقرون بوجود الإله تعالى . والصنبف السابع : اليونانيون، وهم كانوا كالمشعوذين بهذا العلم [ والعمل عليه ] وما طلبوا علماً من العلوم الدقيقة ولا بغوا بحثاً من المباحث النفيسة إلا ليتوسلوا به إلى معرفة [ وجود الإله تعالى ومعرفة ] كبريائه . فهذا هو ضبط [ أصناف ] أهل الدنيا ، وكلهم مطبقون على وجود الإله . والتواريخ القديمة دالة على أن أهل الدنيا من الدهر الداهر ، والزمان الأقدم ، هكذا كانوا وما كان فيهم أحد ينكر وجود الإله تعالى . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن من المعلوم بالضرورة أن عقل [ جميع] أهل المشرق والمغرب في مدة سبعة آلاف سنة ، أو أقل ، أو أكثر : أزيد من عقل واحد مغمور بين الخلق . فعلى هذا لو اتفق لإنسان واحد شبهة ، أوشك في وجود الإله [ تعالى ] فيجب أن يقطع بأن ذلك الشك أو الشبهة لقصور عقله وقلة فهمه ، لا لعدم المطلوب. فإن صريح العقل شاهد بأن عقول جملة الخلق في هذه الدهور المتطاولة ، كانت أكمل من عقل هذا الواحد . فهذا طريق قوي جلي في إثبات العلم بوجود الإله الحكيم ، بشرط أن يعترف الإنسان بان عقله أقل من عقل الكل .
And the sixth category: the Romans, the Berbers, the Copts, and the Abyssinians; the majority of these are Christian. Among them are Muslims. In any case, they affirm the existence of God Almighty. And the seventh category: the Greeks; they were like magicians with this knowledge and practice, and they sought no precise science nor pursued any valuable inquiry except as a means to know the existence of God Almighty and to know His majesty. So this is an enumeration of the categories of the people of the world, and all of them are in agreement upon the existence of God. And ancient histories indicate that the people of the world from time immemorial and the earliest times were thus, and there was among them no one who denied the existence of God Almighty. And if this is established, we say: It is necessarily known that the intellect of all the people of the East and West over a period of seven thousand years, or less, or more, is greater than the intellect of a single individual who is obscure among creation. Therefore, if a single person happens to have a doubt or uncertainty about the existence of God Almighty, it must be concluded that that doubt or uncertainty is due to deficiency in his intellect and lack of his understanding, not due to the absence of what is sought. For sound reason testifies that the intellects of all creation in these long ages were more perfect than the intellect of this one individual. So this is a strong and clear way to establish knowledge of the existence of the Wise God, on condition that the person acknowledges that his intellect is less than the intellect of all.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of God based on universal consensus. It begins by listing two categories of nations—the sixth (Romans, Berbers, Copts, Abyssinians) and the seventh (Greeks)—all of whom affirm God's existence. The author notes that even those who are not Muslims, such as Christians and Greeks, agree on this point. After compiling this historical consensus, he argues that since the collective intellect of all humanity over thousands of years is far greater than that of any single individual, if one person doubts God's existence, the doubt must stem from that individual's intellectual deficiency, not from any lack of evidence. The proof concludes by emphasizing that sound reason confirms the superiority of collective reason, making this a compelling argument for God's existence, provided the doubter admits his own intellectual limitation.

Key Terms
إله
شبهة
عقل
معلوم بالضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this argument relies purely on majority opinion, but it is a logical inference from the premise that collective intellect is more powerful than any individual intellect.
Correction: The argument is not a mere appeal to popularity; it uses the epistemological principle that many minds over time are more reliable than one.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the reason for an individual's doubt about God's existence in light of universal consensus?
A) The evidence for God is unclear.
B) The individual's intellect is deficient.
C) The individual has not studied history.
D) God has hidden His existence from some people.

Key Takeaway
The universal historical consensus on God's existence, combined with the principle of collective intellectual superiority, provides a strong rational argument that individual doubt is due to personal deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted God's existence? According to this argument, humility before the collective intellect of all humanity demands that you question your doubt, not the evidence. Will you accept that your doubt may be a sign of intellectual limitation, or will you cling to it out of pride?
?
If you find yourself unconvinced by this argument, are you willing to admit that your intellect is less than that of all the believers who came before you? What would it cost you to humble yourself in this way?
?
Imagine standing before God on the Day of Judgment. The unanimous testimony of all nations throughout history affirms Him. What excuse will you give if you chose to reject that testimony based on your own solitary reasoning? How does that accountability change how you view your doubts today?
